II. THE PROJECT
A. Rationale for IDA Involvement
Lessons of Past Experience
2.1     Transport infrastructure in Honduras has improved significantly over the last four decades,
to a network of highways and feeder roads that covers most of the country, and a port system that is
adequate. Substantial institutional development has taken place during the period, and management
capabilities in the highway and port subsectors have been improved. These major infrastructure and
institutional developments are largely the result ofT eight highway projects and three ports projects
funded by Bank/IDA (Annex 8). Implementation of the highway projects started in 1955 with a
Highway Maintenance Project, and ended in 1986 with the closing of the Eighth Highway Project.
Port projects started in 1966 with the Puerto Cortes project and ended in 1984 with the closing of the
Third Port Project.
2.2     The Bank/lDA participation has been instrumental in developing the Honduran transport
system. In the highway subsector there was a logical and well designed sequence of project
components throughout the eight loans. The first projects concentrated on construc.ion of major
sections of highways, and development of a road maintenance capacity. The emphasis then turned to
secondary and feeder roads, improving access to and from rural areas. Institution building was
emphasized through several technical assistance programs. While there was some progress in
maintenance planning and operations, results were often described as below expectations. In the ports
subsector, after a successful first project providing Puerto Cortes with much needed modern facilities,
the subsequent projects, because of optimistic demand projections, created excess capacity in Puerto
Castilla and Puerto San Lorenzo. However, institutional objectives were accomplished successfully
and ENP became a well run and relatively autonomous enterprise.
2.3     Past Bank/IDA lending for the transport sector focused on specific investments with limited
regard to the sectoral conte.a. Key policy elements such as intersectoral resource allocation and cost
recovery were not given the importance they deserved. The strategy for the transport sector that
would be supported under the proposed credit would have a sectoral approach with a focus on
resource allocation issues, such as user charges for the funding of investment and maintenance,
adequacy of institutional arrangements and improvement of analytical capacity and achievement of a
better private-public mix to ensure transport efficiency. Legal, regulatory and institutional reforms to
improve transport management already under way indicate the Government's commitment to reform
and should ensure that the proposed program to increase sector efficiency and involve the private
sector is implementable. With this strategy, the risks of investing scarce resources in a distorted
environment will be minimized and measurement of investment priorities will be improved.
2.4     Finally, Bank experience has shown that the development of planning capabilities should
move away from the preparation of massive and highly sophisticated Master Transport Plans that
rapidly become outdated, and toward providing the basic tools for dynamic planning. Delivery of the
technical assistance should be based on participatory approaches in which the recipients have an
important role in identifying their needs and carrying out their planning tasks, with training provided
on day-to-day specific problems. The design draws upon the experience and lessons learned under
previous Bank/IDA loans and credits to Honduras' transport sector, which show that institution
building is a slow process and requires continued support to strengthen transport sector management.



- 19 -
In addition to these country-specific lessons, Bank experience worldwide indicates the need to address
the following types of issues: increased accountability in the government agencies; a better public-
private sector mix; a strong regulatory framework especially in to those areas in which there are clear
market failures; and pricing and investment policies to ensure an economy free of distortions. These
findings are fully addressed in the policy and institutional development components of the project
(paras. 2.9 and 2.20).
Ratio-ale of the Proposed Sector Project
2.5     IDA's lending strategy for Honduras aims to support private-sector led growth and help the
Government develop the appropriate institutional and policy framework within key sectors, at a time
when the Government is showing strong commitment to sector reform. IDA's strategy for the
transport sector seeks to: (a) support institutional and policy changes designed to encourage private
sector participation in transport; (b) strengthen investment selection procedures to ensure that they are
based on sound technical, economic and environmental criteria; (c) help maintain and rehabilitate
existing assets (maintenance and rehabilitation have been shown to yield higher economic returns than
new investments); and (d) promote more thorough assessment of the environmental and efficiency
implications of transport investment and pricing policies. Building on the Government's successful
ongoing structural and sectoral adjustment program, IDA is in a unique position to support the
Government's efforts to restructure the transport sector and help coordinate the efforts of other
institutions, such as IDB and ICAO, which also have an important support role in the development of
sector policies.
2.6     The proposed sector credit broadens the past Bank/IDA strategy of project-specific lending
to embrace sector-wide concerns. The credit would support policy and institutional reforms in the
transport sector and finance a percentage of the sector's investment program. The purpose of the
policy and institutional improvements to be supported under the proposed project is to redefine the
state's role in transport by transferring to the private sector those services that it can better provide,
and to strengthen the capability of the public sector for transport management. The new public-
private mix would reduce the demand on the Government's fiscal resources and prevent the
deterioration of existing investments during periods of public funding constraints, and thus contribute
to sustainable investments in the sector.
B. Project Objectives
2.7     Consistent with IDA's lending strategy, the project has two major objectives: (a) to help
develop the institutional and regulatory framework of the sector so as to: (i) encourage greater
private provision of transport services; (ii) establish investment and pricing policies based on
efficiency and equity considerations; (iii) strengthen SECOPT's capability to discharge its planning
and regulatory functions; (iv) integrate into SECOPT's work the systematic consideration of
environmental concerns; (v) restructure the oort subsector and redefine the role of ENP; (vi) design
and implement a strategy for the development of the airport subsector; and (vii) rationalize railway
operations; and (b) help improve and rehabilitate transport infrastructure in the main trade corridors
in support of the Government's efforts for export-led growth, and reduce the backlog of deferred
maintenance. It is designed as a sector investment project with strong policy-based objectives aimed



- 20 -
at improving sector performance by strengthening its managerial and planning capabilities and
stimulating competition and private sector involvement.
C. Project Description
2.8     Project Description: The project would have three components, as follows: I. Policy
component: The project would assist in further developing and supporting ongoing measures to
increase private sector participation in transport and to implement regulatory and pricing policies
aiming at improving competition and cost recovery in each subsector. A Sector Policy Statement
provides the GOH's medium-term strategy for the sector and related Action Plans, to be supported
under the project, specify measures to be taken to address important issues in each subsector. These
plans would aim, in particular, at: (i) designing and implementing an appropriate regulatory
framework for urban and inter-urban road transport, including the revision of the existing Transport
Law; (ii) increasing the role of the private sector in road maintenance; (iii) implementing sound
systems for road user charger and axle load controls; and (iv) designing and implementing measures
to reorganize the port, airport and railway subsectors. II. The investment component (US$79.6
million) would finance a time slice of the CGovernment's 1993-1996 TSIP totalling US$545.7 million
and would support: (i) rehabilitation of about 150 km of roads in the main export corridors (24% of
total IDA financed project cost); (ii) periodic maintenance of about 1800 km of the rod network
(25%); (iii) construction and rehabilitation of about 1000 m of bridges (7%); (iv) rehabilitation of
1000 km of feeder roads in agriculturally rich areas (21 %); and (v) rehabilitation of the runway,
taxiway and apron of the San Pedro Sula airport (19%). The first year program includes the
rehabilitation of the Choluteca-Guasaule road (44 km) and the Tela-La Ceiba road (100 km). HI. The
institutional development and technical assistance component (US$3.3 million, 4% of IDA financed
total cost) comprises consulting services for: (a) implementing the Government's Institutional
Development Program for: (i) strengthening the capabilities of SECOPT's SSPF and DGT to carry
out project evaluation and implement a regulatory framework consistent with the new public-private
sector mix; and (ii) expanding the role of the Environmental Unit to encompass all transport modes
and effectively integrating environmental aspects into SECOPT's investment decisions; (b) supporting
the implementation of the Action Plans in the areas related to privatization/divestiture measures for
the port and airport subsectors; and (c) strengthening SECOPT's capacity for bridge management.
The following paragraphs provide further details on the project components.
Policy Component
2.9     The close and active policy dialogue on transport issues that IDA and the Government
maintained throughout project preparation has helped to identify and give impetus to important sector
reforms. During 1991 and 1992, to address issues that IDA had previously identified as impeding
sector development, the Government: (a) increased road maintenance by contract, reduced DGCCA
staff and auctioned maintenance equipment to the private sector; (b) approved, through ENP's Board
of Directors, a detailed program for decentralizing port administration and concessioning operations to
local and private interests; (c) agreed with ICAO on a comprehensive technical assistance program for
commercializing and privatizing airport operations and a start on redefining the institutional and
regulatory framework for the subsector; and (d) carried out an in-depth evaluation of the railways
sector options, closed La Ceiba Railway Line, eliminating from FNH's payroll all its operational



-21 -
staff, and approved a plan for phasing out FNH operations. In connection with the proposed project,
the Government and IDA, agreed during negotiations: (i) on the Transport Sector Policy Declaration
presenting the Government's medium-term strategy for the sector; (ii) on the implementation of the
Action Plans to address the key sector issues according to the timetables set forth in the Action Plans,
including, jn  alia, the revision of the present Transport Law and related regulations (these Action
Plans are summarized in paras. 2.22 to 2.27 and contained in Annex 1); and (iii) that it would be an
event of default if the Government takes any action or adopts any policy within the sector not
consistent with the policies outlined in the Transport Sector Policy Declaration (para. 3.1 (e)).
During the annual and mid-term project reviews (para. 2.34) IDA and the Government would jointly
evaluate the progress made in implementing the Policy Declaration and related Action Plans and agree
on any necessary adjustments.
Investment Component
2.10    The US$77.5 million investment component of the project would finance a time slice of the
Government's 1993-1996 TSIP totalling US$545.7 million. The proposed IDA credit would finance
selected rehabilitation, periodic maintnance and improvement works of this program. The level of
expenditures in the TSIP is considered adequate to attend to high priority construction and
rehabilitation works in order to upgrade the road network to maintainable conditions, provide for a
reduction of the backlog of deferred rood maintenance and address the unmet needs in the airport
subsector. Table 2.1 summarizes the 1993-1996 TSIP. During negotiations agreement was reached
on: (i) the 1993-1996 TSIP; and (ii) that any change to the 1993-1996 TSIP, which would be
reviewed during the annual and mid-term reviews to update its scope and content in view of progress
achieved in the previous year and possible changes in priority, would have to be satisfactory to IDA
(paras. 2.34 and 3.1(f)).
2.11    The investments in the transport sector would help improve and maintain Honduras'
infrastructure to support both export-led growth and regional economic integration. The main
investments included in the program are: (a) in the hif,ay subse , construction of nearly 1,500
km of roads, mostly feeder roads; improvement (widening) of 86 km of the main road network to
relieve congestion on the roads links connecting San Pedro Sula to Puerto Cortes and to the
neighboring industrial parks, and the road between San Pedro Sula and Progreso; paving of 587 km
of the secondary road network; conEtruction and rehabilitation of bridges; the rehabilitation of nearly
2,500 km of roads including main connections with Nicaragua (i.e. Jicaro Galan-EI Espino and
Choluteca-Guasaule), with Guatemala (Cbamalecon-Entrada), and with El Salvador (La Paz-
Goascaran); and road maintenance works. About 56% of road works included in the program have
already been initiated (Table 6 in Annex 3); (b) in the aor se , rehabilitation of the runway
and construction of a new passenger and freight trminal at the San Pedro Sula airport, and
construction of a passenger terminal in Roatan; and (c) in the VWubsetr expansion of the
container terminal and constrution of a Ro-Ro facility at Puerto Cortes.

- 23 -
2.12    No investment with an economic rate of return (ERR) below 12% and/or which is not
environmentally sound, both evaluated using methods and parameters satisfactory to IDA, would be
included or subsequently entered into the 1993-1996 program, except for a limited number of
investments in access roads (not to exceed 10% of the total expenditures in access roads) that have an
exceptionally strong social impact in terms of improved access to social services and employment.
The economic efficiency criteria would be relaxed for these investments, but they should still meet
standard environmental criteria. These projects must meet the following conditions: (a) new roads
would not exceed 14 km in length; and (b) construction of access roads would not exceed US$650 per
capita of the population served1. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations (para.
3.1(g)).
2.13    The overall size of the TSIP, approximately US$136 million per year corresponds to about
4% of current GDP, i.e. some 20% below the annual average 5% of GDP allocated to the sector
during the 1980s. The program represents a substantial shift from construction to rehabilitation and
maintenance, which is appropriate, particularly considering the past neglect of the road transport
infrastructure and the need to upgrade airport facilities. The relative amounts allocated to the various
modes also correctly reflect their economic roles and needs: about 74% of the total is for highways,
13% for airports, 3% for ports, and 10% for technical assistance and studies. For highways, about
29% of expendituees will be for maintenance, 38% for road and bridge rehabilitation, and 33% for
road construction, improvement and paving. The allocation of about 61 % of road funds for the
rehabilitation and maintenance of the network correctly reflects the high priority of the administration
is giving to the protection of the existing capital stock. The capability of the contracting industry to
carry out road rehabilitation and maintenance was reviewed during appraisal and found satisfactory.
2.14    The investments in road improvement, rehabilitation and periodic maintenance for the 1993-
1995 program (see Annex 3) are based on carefully analyzed economic feasibility and priority
ranking. For 1996 they are based on estimates that take into consideration maintenance needs for the
rehabilitated road network. These needs have been identified through the evaluation of alternative
maintenance policies in order to minimize both maintenance expenditures and vehicle operating costs.
Construction of passenger and freight terminals for the San Pedro Sula Airport are based on an
Airport Development Plan carried out by a Spanish Consultant firm with financing from the
Government of Spain. Construction, on a turnkey basis, is also being negotiated with a Spanish firm.
About 65% of the cost of these airport investments is for equipment and the Government is reviewing
ways to reduce these costs. In particular, SECOPIT is reevaluating the need for primary and
secondary radars, and for loading and unloading equipment for the freight terminal-such equipment
could be provided by the future private operator. The proposed airport investments will fill an
important gap in the transport infrastructure of Honduras, but further investments will be needed to
modernize the country's airport services and provide an acceptable standard of efficiency and
security. The most obvious need is to replace the unsafe Toncontin Airport in Tegucigalpa, and
alternative sites would be studied as part of ICAO's supported Airport Development Plan.
Meanwhile, to attend the growing demand for air cargo, the military has agreed on the use of

2.15    Main Road Network Rehabilitation. IDA financing would include road rehabilitation and
improvement of about 150 km of roads in the main export corridors. During the first year, the
project would finance the rehabilitation of the Choluteca-Guasaule road (two lanes, 44 km), part of
the so-called Central America "natural" land corridor which provides the best road connection
between the countries of the region. The use of this road by traffic to and from Nicaragua was
interrupted following the destruction of the Guasaule bridge in 1982. A temporary one-lane bridge
has been built: construction of the permanent bridge started this year with financing from the
European Economic Community. The current traffic of 1,500 vehicles per day (vpd) is expected to
increase to 2,000 vpd by the year 2000. The road rehabilitation feasibility study, carried out in early
1992 by local consultants with financing provided by the Central American Bank for Economic
Integration (CABEI), concluded that the pavement and shoulders are in poor condition and that
immediate rehabilitation is needed to prevent further deterioration. Final engineering and
environmental studies have already been completed. The expected ERR for these works is 21 %.
Also during the first year, the project would finance the rehabilitation of the Tela-La Ceiba road (two
lanes), which is part of the main east-west corridor in northern Honduras. In addition to connecting
these two major urban centers (ports), its importance is linked to the agriculturally rich land in that
part of the country. The feasibility study conducted in early 1992 by a local consultant firm shows
that the road surface is in fair condition requiring only a sealing coat to prevent the pavement from
deteriorating. Some sections of this road with total length of about 10 km require
reconstruction/rehabilitation to correct faults in the pavement structure or destruction caused by
landslides. The major investment on this road will be directed to the rehabilitation of 15 bridges
damaged by the changing conditions in the river flows. The road is located in a hydrologically
difficul area where debris carried by rivers sediments near the bridges producing changes on the river
bed and thus in the river flow. This issue will be addressed through appropriate river course
treatments and bridge designs. Engineering and environmental studies have already been completed.
The expected EER for these works is also 21 %.
2.16    Feeder Roads Network Rehabilitation Program. This program would include rehabilitation
of about 1000 km of feeder roads. The roads would be selected on the basis of their individual merit
based on a minimum ERR of 12% and technical criteria that have been reviewed by IDA and found
satisfactory. The roads would give the rural poor access to the markets for their products, enabling
them to improve their living standards. Once rehabilitated, the roads would be transferred to
DGCCA for inclusion in the Directorate's road maintenance program.
2.17    Road Maintenance. IDA financing would include periodic maintenance of about 1800 km
of roads through the award of four-year contracts for the maintenance of the road network in a
subregion of the country. This approach is expected to yield the following benefits: (a) lower costs
due to the guarantee of continuous work for the contractor; and (b) improved road maintenance by
"locking-in" funds for that purpose. SECOPT is expected to finalize in June 1993 the design of the
scheme, particularly: (a) building in the contracts a workable definition of standards on which the
performance of the contractor would be monitored; and (b) defining the Government's and the
contractors' responsibilities. IDA financing of this project component would be on a declining basis
(para. 2.40(b)), and it would allow the necessary lerd time for the Government to design and
gradually implement a Road Fund with IDB assistance.
2.18    Bridge Rehabilitation Program. The project would include construction and rehabilitation of
approximately 1000 linear meters (Im) of bridges on the primary road network. The final selection of



- 26 -
the subprojects to be included in this program would be completed during the first year of project
implementation. As part of the technical assistance for the design and implementation of a bridge
management system (para. 2.19(c)) the consultants will screen and prioritize the subprojects identified
by DGC as requiring urgent rehabilitation on the basis of technical parameters related to traffic loads
and degree of deterioration of the bridges.
2.19    Runway and Taxiway Rehabilitation - San Pedro Sula Airport. This component would
include the complete rehabilitation of the San Pedro Sula Airport's 2,700-meter runway, its taxiway
and apron, which are in poor condition. The works includes improvement of drainage and provision
of pumping equipment. The proposed design was prepared by the US Corps of Engineers in 1989.
The rehabilitation was supported by a full economic analysis conducted by a local firm in early 1992.
This subproject has an estimated ERR of 20%.
Institutional Development and Technical Assistance Component
2.20    This US$3.3 million ccmponen: comprises technical assistance and training to strengthen
SECOPT's capacity to plan and regulate and to improve bridge management, and to implement the
Action Plans for reorganizing the ports and airports. In particular, the project would include:
(a)  Technical Assistance and Training to SECOPT. To implement SECOPT's Institutional
Development Program, consultant services would provide technical assistance and
training to: (a) strengthen the capacity of SSPF to plan, program and budget, and to
incorporate environmental considerations into the planning process; and (b) to redefine
DGT's role and improve its capacity to analyze pricing and regulatory policies, as well
as to develop a suitable data base on land transport (Annex 2).
(b) Technical Assistance to Implement the Action Plans. The project would finance
consultants to Xti!st i reorganizing the port and airport subsectors, in areas related to
concession of assvis t-i the private sector. In particular, this component would finance
international experts ca evaluation of concessioning alternatives in the port subsector,
asset valuation, concession contracts, bidding documents and the preparation of
business plans and. in the case of airports, the co-management of the San Pedro Sula
and Toncontin airports for a period of eighteen months. Terms of reference for the
technical assistance to the IC are included in Annex 7; and for the technical assistance
for airport management, and its relation to ICAO's technical assistance, in Annex 6;
and
(c) Design and Imolementation of a Bridge Management System. Consultant services to
design and implement a bridge management system in the DGC's Bridge Department.
Technical assistance would include purchase of computing equipment to implement the
system. The consultants would also recommend and help implement the required
organizational changes to ensure that the system is successfully brought into use and
operated. Terms of reference for this technical assistance are included in Annex 4.
2.21    During negotiations, agreement was reached on the final Terms of Reference for: (i) the
technical assistance to implement the Institutional Development Plan; (ii) implementation of the Action
Plans in the port and airport subsectors; and (iii) establishing a Bridge Management System in



- 27 -
SECOPT (para. 3.1(h)). Implementation of the technical assistance, dates of completion, and follow-
up actions are included in the Government's Action Plans and would be monitored during the annual
and mid-term reviews (para. 2.34). During negotiations agreement was reached that: (i) appointment
of consultants to carry out the technical assistance for the evaluation of the economic and financial
impact of different leasing/concessioning alternatives in the port subsector would be a condition of
credit effectiveness (para. 3.2(c)); (ii) hiring consultants for the implementation of port reforms
would take place not later than October 31, 1993 (para. 3.1(i)); (iii) appointment of consultants to
carry out the technical assistance for airport management would be a condition of disbursement for
the rehabilitation of the runway in the San Pedro Sula airport (para. 3.3); and (iv) the consultants to
implement the Bridge Management System would start work prior to October 31, 1993 (para. 3.1(i)).
For the consultant services described in para. 2.20 (a) agreement was reached at negotiations that:
(ii) engagement of the consultants to carry out the first phase of the Program and initiation of their
work should take place no later than July 31, 1993 and appointment of the consultants to implement
the Second phase of the program would take place not later than December 15, 1993. Table 2.3
summarizes the technical assistance component of the project, the implementation schedule and

D. The Action Plans
Actions to be Taken
2.22    The main sectoral issues were identified in discussions with the Government during the
preparation of IDA's Transport Sector Strategy Paper for Honduras (Report No. 19285-HO of April
10, 1992). Based upon the report's findings, the Govermment selected a series of priority actions and
incorporated them into action plans covering policy and institutional reforms, as detailed in Annex 1
and summarized below. Most of the reforms in the highway subsector will be carried out with IDB's
financed technical assistance under their ongoing project. IDB and IDA have closely collaborated to
help the Government of Honduras develop a comprehensive policy for the road subsector. The
inclusion in the Action Plans of those measures to be implemented with IDB's support, provides IDA
a vehicle for a continuous dialogue with the Government and the IDB on the full range of key road
subsector issues such as road pricing, axle-load controls and DGCCA's development of performance
budgets to strengthen accountability. Progress in implementing the Action Plans would be monitored
during project supervision through annual and mid-term reviews (para. 2.34).
2.23    At the institutional level, reforms would aim to: strengthen SECOPT's capacity to plan and
regulate and to integrate environmental concerns into sector management; and (ii) revise the present
regulatory framework. The main actions contemplated to attain these objectives are:
(a)  develop five-year rolling Transport Investment Plans;
(b) strengthen the planning capabilities of SECOPT, SSPF, and the modal agencies by
introducing the necessary planning methodologies and providing tools and staff
training;
(c) develop DGT's capacity to carry out its regulatory functions by upgrading
methodologies for the evaluation of urban transport pricing, strengthening policy
analysis, and developing the necessary data base including on vehod:, fleet,
composition, age, and safety;
(d) expand the role of the Environmental Unit in SECOPT to encompass all transport
modes;
(e) develop environmental guidelines for all transport modes and the necessary standards
to measure the environmental impact of various transport projects;
(f) revise the present Transport Law and related regulations in order to increase
competition in freight transport; and
(g) target urban transport subsidies to the lowest income groups.
2.24    In the hiLhway subhctor, the objectives are to:



- 30 -
(a) increase SRCOPT's accountability for road maintenance through the development of
performance budgets;
(b)  develop SECOPT's capacity for contract management;
(c) strengthen DGC's and DGCCA's engineering standards by developing appropriate
manuals;
(d) review the present system of axle-load controls and, based on the results, implement
an enforceable system that would ensure Honduras' compliance with Central American
agreements on vehicle weights and dimensions; and
(e) develop a system for monitoring road user charges and the mechanism to adjust
charges when needed.
2.25    In the port subsector, reforms would aim to improve efficiency in the provision of port
services through the promotion of competition and greater private-sector participation in selected port
activities, and strengthen investment decisions. These objectives would be attained through the
following proposed actions:
(a) transferring operational responsibility for the port of La Ceiba to the Municipal
Government or the local Chamber of Commerce, concession Puerto San Lorenzo ard
Puerto Castilla to private operators and transfer to the private sector selected
areas/activities in Puerto Cortes. This action would include preparation of financial
and personnel plans for ENP, revision of tariffs and revision of legal aspects of
changes;
(b) retain a team of consultants to help ENP to transfer the selected port activities to the
private sector; inspection and classification of the installation and equipment to be
subject to concession, lease or sale; and preparation of bidding documents;
(c) establishi cost-based tariffs in the port system; and
(d) set up a separate administration for the Puerto Cortes container terminal to permit the
terminal to operate as an independent unit.
2.26    The objectives for the civil aviation subsector are to 2':
(a) reorganize and strengthen DGAC capacity to carry out the responsibilities vested in it
by law, including design and implementation of an organizational structure for the
subsector to permit the airports to operate on a commercial basis;
(b) design and implement operational procedures for the airports (cost centers, billing,
etc.);
2/ Most of thews objectives will be pursued under the UNDP financed SECOPT-ICAO program for Civil Aviation
Dovelopment.



- 31 -
(c)  prepare a National Development Plan for Civil Aviation enabling DGAC to carry out
timely development of the airport infrastructure and services under its jurisdiction,
evaluate and approve investment projects and promote the systematic development of
the activities that the law assigns to it; and
(d)  increase operational efficiency through adequate training for compliance with
internationally accepted norms, methods and procedures and for adequate preventive
maintenance of electronic systems and airport infrastructure.
2.27    The main reforms in the railway subsector aim to:
(a) concession usable assets to interested operators, primarily the Tela Railway Co.; and
(b) phase out remaining operations by 1996 through a detailed program involving the
gradual and orderly reduction of FNH traffic and personnel.
E. Project Cost and Financing
Project Cost
2.28    The total cost of the 1993-1996 TSIP is US$545.7 million including contingencies
(Table 2.1). The base costs are estimated as of June 1992. Foreign costs have been estimated at
62% of the works included in the program. For items A. through E. in Table 2.2, supervision of
works is estimated at 8% of the cost of the works, and a physical contingency of 10% has been
added, which is considered reasonable as none of the works present major risks. The price
contingency assumes that the IDA-financed subprojects would be executed in four years, I.e. two
years less than the LAC profile for similar projects, taking into account DGC's and DGCCA excellent
record in the prompt execution of their ongoing investment program. Price contingencies were
calculated in line with IDA guidelines, which assume international price escalation at a rate of 3.9%
per yeat during 1993 and 1994, and 3.8% thereafter. The rate of escalation for international prices
was also assumed to be applicable to local costs (when expressed in dollar terms) which implies that
the exchange rate is expected to be periodically adjusted to reflect the difference between internal
inflation and the evolution of world prices.
Prject Fnancng
2.29    For specific subprojects amounting to about 80% of the investment program sources of
financing have already been secured and/or identified and 56% of the works have already been
started. IDA would finance selected components of this program, totalling about US$65 million
(Table 2.2). IDB would finance about US$190 million of the road component. Of this amount.
US$110 million correspond to the ongoing MDB operation for which most subprojects have ben
identified, and US$80 million to a proposed IDB follow-on project currently under preparation, which
would focus in the rehabilitation of the secondary road network. CABEI has approved loans for a
total of US$30 million for ongoing works to widen the Northern Highway, and for road rehabilitation
and the replacement of temporary bridges in the Northern plain. USAID is expected to finance
US$20 million for paving secondary roads which already have traffic levels above 350 v.p.d. Road



- 32 -
rehabilitation works financed with a US$20 million loan from the Government of Taiwan, mosdy in
the Juticalpa-Catacamas road serving the Olancho region, have already started and their completion is
programmed for 1994. Negotiations with a Spanish firm for turn-ke" projects in the airports of San
Pedro Sula and Roatan, are already at an advaaced stage and contract signature is expected to take
place in January 1993. Financing from the Spanish Government totalling US$50 million has already
been secured. Table 2.4 summarizes the estimated financing plan for the investment program. Since
most of the financing provided by the donors is already tied to specific subprojects for which there is
no cofinancing, pro ^t implementation would not pose significant problems with respect to financing
and procurement coordination. For the annual updating of the investment program for the road
subsector, and for the implementation of policy and institutional reforms in tht road subsector, IDB
and IDA would continue the close collaboration that existed during the process of project preparation.
2.30    The Central Government contribution to the 1993-1996 TSIP is estimated at US$121
million, or about US$30 million on average per year. This is in line with past allocation of domestic
resources to the sector. Budgetary funding from the Central Government is estimated at US$42
million in 1993, declining to US$35 million in 1994, US$26 million in 1995 and US$18 million in
1996, as the bacldog of road rehabilitation is cleared. The 1993 budget includes the necessary
fimancing. The proposed program does not include possible needed investwments for the replacement
of the Tegucigalpa airport, which may pose a significant additional demand on budgetary resources in
the 1995-1996 period. For the following years, should counterpart funding be less than programmed,
the Government and IDA would agree on investment priorities within the sector during the annual and
mid-term implementation reviews (pdra. 2.34).

2.31    The proposed credit would finance about 12% of the four-year investment program and
78% of the, ^ost of the selected components (see Table 2.2), with the remaining 22% to, be financed
by the Government of Honduras. To avoid delays in commencing project implementation, which
would be costly both in terms of increased infrastructure deterioration and subsequent need for
revising engineering studies, retroactive financing for eligible road rehabilitation and periodic
maintenance expenditures incurred after July 31, 1992 but not before twelve months prior to the
expected date of credit signature would be provided for civil works, up to a maximum amount of
US$1.7 million equivalent.
F. Project Ekecution
Implementation Arrangements
2.32    The Government is expected to execute the proposed project in about four years under its
own responsibility, except for the implementation of the Action Plan for the port subsector which
would be under the responsibility of the IC, SECOPT and ENP, and the Action Plan for the Railways
for which FNH would be responsible (para. 1.44). SECOPT would be responsible for coordinating
overall project implementation, including monitoring the regulatory reforms and the Sector Policy
Declaration, and for the execution of the civil works and technical assistance financed under the
proposed credit. A Project Implementation Unit headed by a full-time Project Coordinator (PC), with
qualifications satisfactory to IDA, would be responsible for monitoring sector developments and for
ensuring that timely action is taken to achieve the objectives of the Sector Policy Declaration and
related Action Plans. The PC, who would directly report to the Vice-Minister of SSPF is expected to
play a key role in coordinating the preparation and prepare, as necessary, progress reports and the
supporting documentation for the anmnal and mid-term reviews that IDA and the Government would
use to supervise project implementation against key monitoring indicators and other criteria (para.
2.34). The PC would be supported by one engineer and one accountant. The Government has
appointed a PC, with qualifications satisfactory to IDA, for the proposed project. Full staffing of the
Project Coordinating Unit would be a condition of credit effectiveness (para. 3.2(d)). SECOPT
would be responsible for implementing the civil works to be financed under the project. For the
airport subsector, SECOPT would assume responsibility for implementing the Action Plan.
Responsibility for the technical assistance program for transport planning and transport policy and
regulations would be assigned, respectively, to SSPF and DGT. During negotiations, agreement was
reached with the Government on the functions and responsibilities of the PC (para. 3.10)).
Project Reporing
2.33    At negotiations, agreement was reached that SECOPT would submit semi-annual progress
reports to IDA, by May 31 and November 30 of each year for the previous semester (para. 3.1(k)).
As important objectives of the project are to support Government reforms to reorganize and privatize
transport operations, remove regulatory barriers that unnecessarily increase the cost of transport, and
achieve the objectives included in the Sector Policy Declaration, the semi-annual progress reports
should review progress in these areas as well as in the implementation of all project components. The
implementing agencies would be responsible for preparing progress and completion reports on their
respective components. The PC would be responsible, with the assistance of the implementing
agencies, for physical and financial monitoring of project activities; and would coordinate preparation



- 34 -
of all reports and consolidate the same for submission to IDA. The Borrower would also be required
to present to IDA a project completion report not later than six months after the closing date of the
credit (para. 3.1(1)).
Annual and Mid-term Reviews
2.34    By no later than June 30 of each year, starting in 1993, IDA and the Government would
conduct formal reviews of progress made in implementing the proposed project. Should these
reviews indicate that progress toward meting the project objectives is unsatisfactory, the Government
would prepare, not later than one month after said review, and execute to IDA's satisfaction, remedial
action plans. The annual reviews would provide an opportunity to assess project performance (and
identify remedial actions) including progress made in: achieving the objectives of the Transport
Sector Policy Declaration and related Action Plans (Annex 1); reorganizing and deregulating the
transport sector and in implementing the Institutional Development Program (Annex 2); the
implementation of the Bridge Management system (Annex 4); the implementation of the investment
program and the updating of the four-year rolling TSIP, including the screening of all projects over
US$3 million to ensure the project's technical and economic soundness; funding arrangements; the
pace of physical project execution; achieving the project monitoring indicators (Annex 9); and
evaluating project management and coordination, in particular: (i) the performance of the PC and
his/her ability to coordinate sector institutions to achieve the project objectives, and (ii) technical
ability and soundness of judgement in the areas of procurement and disbursement. After about two
years of project implementation (by June 30, 1995), IDA and the Government would conduct a mid-
term review of project execution. In addition to covering all areas included in the annual reviews, the
mid-term would pay particular attention to: (a) the overall progress being made in achieving the aims
of the project and of the Government's Transport Sector Policy Declaration; (b) the progress made in
implementing the Action Plans; and (c) compliance with and possible adjustments to the project
monitoring indicators (Annex 9) paying particular attention to: (i) achievement of road maintenance
physical and financial targets and increased accountability for road management; (ii) increased
involvement of the private sector in port operations and the resulting gradual reduction of ENP staff
(Annex 5); (iii) separation of the normative and operating functions of DGCA and transfer of
operation and budgetary responsibilities to airport managers (Annex 6); (iv) gradual, orderly phase-
out of FNH services according to a clearly defined program aimed at discontinuing FNH operations
by 1996 (Annex 7); and (v) revised transport regulations designed to free the trucking industry from
price and entry regulations and to target urban transport subsidies to low income groups. Annex 9
presents further details on the supervision plan, the proposed agenda for the annual and mid-term
reviews, the key monitoring indicators, and the targets dates that will be used to review progress in
implementing the Action Plans and to identify the areas requiring remedial actions. At negotiations,
IDA reached agreement with the Government on the scope and timing of such reviews and its
commitment to the implementation, if necessary, of satisfactory remedial action plans (para. 3. 1(m)).
G. Procurement and Consultancy Services
2.35    Procurement of goods and civil works, would be done in accordance with IDA's Guidelines
for Procurement (May, 1992 version). To the extent practicable, the procurement of goods would be
done bv grouping the various equipment/materials items in bid packages estimated to cost the

equivalent of US$250,000 or more. The contracting of consulting services to be financed under the
Institutional Development Component and for the supervision of civil works would be done in
accordance to IDA's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants (August 1981 version). Details are shown
in Table 2.5.

2.36    Procurement procedures would be as follows:
(a)  bid packages or contracts for the supply of goods estimated to cost US$250,000
equivalent each or more, and contracts for civil works estimated to cost BS$1 million
equivalent each or more, would be procured following ICB procedures in accordance
with IDA's Guidelines. Procurement for civil works estimated to cost more than the
equivalent of US$5 million per contract, would be subject to pre-qualification of
contractors under procedures satisfactory to IDA. In ICB procurement of goods,
regional, including domestic manufacturers would be granted the margin of preference
accorded to members of the Central American Common Market. In ICB procurement
of civil works, domestic contractors would be granted a margin of preference of 7.5%
in accordance with the procedures set forth in para. 5 of Appendix 2 of IDA
Guidelines;
(b)  civil works of less than US$1 million per contract up to an aggregate amount of
US$24.6 million, equivalent would be procured through LCB procedures satisfactory
to IDA. Foreign bidders would be given an equal opportunity to compete under LCB
if they wish to do so;
(c)  all contracts for goods estimated to cost the equivalent of US$250,000 or more, all
contracts for works estimated to cost the equivalent of US$1 million or more, and the
first LCB contract for goods and for works would be subject to IDA's prior review in
accordance with paras. 2 and 4 of the IDA's procurement Guidelines. Overall, IDA
review is expected to cover no less than 72% of total contract values, which is
satisfactory; and



- 36 -
(d) contracts for ancillary equipment required for the Institutional Development
Component (PC computers, software, office equipment, training equipment, and a
small number of vehicles) estimated to cost less than US$25,000 and up to an
aggregate amount of US$0.12 million will be awarded following local/international
shopping procedures based on comparing price quotations received from at least three
eligible suppliers. Although contracts for goods larger than US$25,000 are not
expected for this component, contracts exceeding US$25,000 but below US$250,000
would be procured through LCB procedures acceptable to IDA.
2.37    Agreement to the above procurement arrangements including the use of standard bidding
documents was reached during negotiations (para. 3.1(n)). To minimize possible adverse impacts on
the environment during the implementation of the civil works, the bidding documents specifications
would include clauses under which the contractors would be required to prevent, minimize or mitigate
environmental damage in line with the recommendations contained in the Eas. A recent assessment
by IDA of existing procurement procedures in Honduras indicates that they are not fully compatible
with IDA Guidelines. The procedures unacceptable to IDA are remedied through provisions included
in the Credit Agreement which would be applicable to the proposed project.
H. Disbursements and Auditing
2.38    The proposed credit is expected to be fully disbursed by June 30, 1997 and the closing date
would be December 31, 1997. The disbursement schedule (Table 2.6) is two years less than the
standard profile applicable to Honduras, reflecting the excellent record of SECOPT in implementing
Bank/!DA financed projects and the time-slice cha acter of the proposed operation. Annex 10 shows
the estimated quarterly disbursement schedule.
2.39    Disbursements would be administered by SECOPT. Disbursements would be against
periodic statement of expenditures (SOE), except in those cases when prior IDA review of
procurement documentation is required. In order to speed up disbursements, a special account in US
dollars with an authorized allocation of US$4 million (sufficient for about four months' worth of
financeable expenditures) would be established in the Banco Central de Honduras. SECOPT would
be entitled to make periodic withdrawals from the special account at the exchange rate applicable on
the day the special account is debited. IDA would replenish the special account for the amount of
withdrawals on account of eligible expenditures at the request of the Borrower.
2.40    Disbursements would be made on the following basis:
(a) 80% of expenditures on: (i) rehabilitation of trunk roads; (ii) construction and
rehabilitation of bridges; (iii) rehabilitation of the runway in the San Pedro Sula
Airport; and (iv) rehabilitation of feeder roads;
(b) 80% of expenditures on periodic road maintenance until an aggregate amount of
US$4.8 million equivalent has been disbursed; 60% of expenditures on periodic road
maintenance until an aggregate amount of US$8.4 has been disbursed; and 45%
thereafter;



- 37 -
(c) 100% of expenditures on consulting services for technical assistance and supervision;
and
(d) 100% of foreign expenditures for imported equipment and 80% of local expenditures.

2.41 At negotiations, agreement was reached that SECOPT would maintain separate accounts for
each of the project components. SECOPT would maintain adequate documentation for SOE's
(para. 2.39) which would be subject to annual audits. The special account at the Banco Central of
Honduras would also be audited once a year. All audits would be carried out by independent auditors
acceptable to IDA and in accordance with terms of reference to be approved by IDA. Audit reports
would be furnished to IDA within five months after the close of the Government's fiscal year (para.
3. 1 (o)).
1. Economic Justification
2.42 The project is expected to support the introduction of major policy and structural reforms and
help strengthening the sector's institutions through the implementation of the various Action Plans
covering a wide range of policy and institutional improvements. By addressing sector specific issues
and overriding institutional weaknesses, the project would contribute to enhance Honduras
comparative advantages in interoational trade and substantially reduce transport related distortions in
the economy. Direct benefits of the project would be derived from: (a) increasing the quality and
cost-effectiveness of transport services through: (1) improving the efficiency and accountability in the
road subsector; (ii) making ports and airport services more responsive to user needs; and (iii)
eliminaing intermodal distortions by phasing out railway services; (b) strengthening sector planning
and policy formulation; and (c) improving the sustanability of resource use through the development
of a technical foundation for an environmentally sound transport arrangement. The project's benefits
would therefore go well beyond the reurns on specific individual investments.
2.43 Due to the difficulty of preparing quantitative estimates related to public sector policy and
institutional reforms, no economic rate of return could be calculated for the policy and institutional
development components of the project. However, the project would bring significant direct benefits
10/   Correpond to the initial US$4 millian deposit in the Special Account plus retroactive financing of up to
US$1.7 million for oeble expendtume inurrd after July 31, 1992.



- 38 -
in terms of increased transport sector productivity, while generating public sector net savings
conservatively estimated at about US$20 million per year from: (a) reduced cost of road maintenance
by contracting works with the private sector; (b) reformulation of urban transport subsidies; and (c)
elimination of FNH's deficit.
2.44 The economic viability of about 75% of the investments included in the 1993-1996 TSIP has
been estimated, yielding EER's ranging from 12% to above 50%, except for a small amount of
construction of access roads with a strong social impact (para. 2.12). The analysis of works in the
road rehabilitation and periodic maintenance component was based on a detailed inventory of the
existing paved roads and the results obtained from the application of the HDM m. About 259 links
totalling 5,546 km were evaluated, of which 2,400 km were identified for rehabilitation, including
those proposed to be financed under the project. Because of lack of timely maintenance, these roads
are entering the critical period when deterioration accelerates. The maintenance policies used in the
analyses were developed in collaboration with DGC and DGCCA and the unit costs of maintenance
operations used are reasonable for the region. Traffic volumes and compositions are based on actual
counts supplemented with origin/destination data. The EER for these works range from 12% to
above 50%. In the case of feeder roads, the economic justification was based on the evaluation of the
entire network (8,619 km), and more than 1,500 kn were identified for rehabilitation or periodic
maintenance, with ERRs also ranging from 12% to above 50%. The analysis of these roads was
based on the producer surplus method, for which the value of the additional agricultural production
attributed to the reduction in transport costs was quantified. Under the current investment financing
constraints, however, not all feeder road projects with a positive net present value that also meet the
agreed technical and environmental criteria can be executed. The final selection was made on the
basis of the export corridor concept, selecting groups of roads feeding the main traffic arteries.
Investments in the rehabilitation of the airport runway in San Pedro Sula have an estimated EER of
20% . Annex 11 provides further details of the economic evaluation of the first year investment
program.
J. Environmental Impact
2.45 The proposed project's Environmental Category is B. All components eligible for financing
under the proposed credit would be executed by competent contractors, supervised by qualified
engineers and would neither entail significant excavation nor interfere with the fauna or flora. All
contracts for civil works would include appropriate safeguards regarding materials to be used,
disposal of removed material, management of quarries, etc. Also, cleaning of clogged roadside
ditches and drains as part of routine maintenance operations would restore runoff to the designed
channels and reduce erosion. Furthermore, all works under the program are subject, as part of the
feasibility analysis, to environmental impact assessments carried out by local consultants. IDA has
reviewed the capabilities of the local consulting industry and has found them satisfactory. In the case
of the design of bridges in hydrologically difficult areas, international experts with qualifications
satisfactory to IDA would be retained to ensure sound environmental treatment of river courses.
Also, the credit would help strengthen the Environmental Unit in SECOPT, centralizing the
responsibility and capacity for environmental assessments in the sector and providing it with the
necessary resources to monitor the implementation of the recommended mitigating actions. T!he
project would not finance construction of new roads, or works that would lead to population



- 39 -
displacements. IDA has been provided with satisfactory EAs for the road rehabilitation works to be
financed under the first year program.
K. Project Risks and Safeguards
2.46 The proposed project does not pose any significant technical risks. A detailed first year
program of works was prepared by SECOPT and appraised by IDA. As the works to be financed
under the project are technically simple - rehabilitation and periodic maintenance- the quantities are
not expected to change substantially from the engineering estimates. Because of the project's policy
emphasis, the main risks are related to the Government's sustained commitment to the needed
institutional reforms, and its ability to muster the political support to implement them, particularly in
the roads and civil aviation subsector, where the issues are the most pressing. These risks are
mitigated by the significant up-front actions, such as closure of the La Ceiba railway line, signature of
the UNDP-ICAO-SECOPT agreement, and substantial reductions in DGCCA's staff that have already
been taken. To address the potential risk that the next administration may falter in carrying out the
agreed reforms, the project arrangements provide for joint annual progress reviews by the
Government and IDA to enable IDA to continue to have an effective dialogue for implementation of
the reforms. Any significant policy reversals would be an event of default (para. 3.1(e)). The risk
that SECOPT might have insufficient capacity to implement a complex project involving substantial
sector reforms was taken into account in the design of an intensive supervision plan and the inclusion
under the project of short-term technical assistance to support SECOPT in those areas, such as
concession arrangements, which require special expertise. Finally, the risk of funding shortages
cannot be discounted, and may lead to slower than planned implementation of the investment
program. Annual and mid-term reviews will provide the opportunity to review project priorities in
light of possible budgetary constraints and ensure that subprojects with the highest economic returns
are executed first.

GOVERNNT OF THE REPUTBLIC OF HONDURAS
SECRETARIAT OF COMMUNICATIONS, PUBLIC WORKS AND TRANSPORT
(SECOPI)
Policy Declaration and Action Plan for the
Transport Sector
1.    Macroeconomic Considerations
The Government of Honduras has adopted a macroeconomic plan, which has been
implemented through an economic adjustment program. The objectives of this program have
been to foster efficiency and effectiveness in the administration of the Government's
resources, as well as to establish fiscal discipline in the public sector and reduce significantly
the budget deficit. This initiative was enacted as law in the filrst months of our
administration, when our Congress approved the "Law of Structural Adjustment of the
Economy" (Decree 18-90).
As a matter of policy, the Govermnent envisages the participation of the private sector
in all those activities that have been traditionally carried out by the public sector, but where
the private sector can achieve greater efficiency. The Government also seeks to promote
investment and encourage the growth of our exports so that our economy can grow in a
sustainable fashion and benefit all our population, particularly those who traditionally have
benefited the least.
As part of its economic policy, the Government has begun a decentralization and,
whenever pertinent, a deregulation process. This will provide the enabling environment for
the private sector to grow and compete freely, and whenever this is not possible because
natural monopoly conditions are encountered, it will also lead to a transparent and equitable
regulatory framework.
In another area, our Administration has made important advances to redefine the role
of government, by restricting it to those functions that naturally correspond to it. In this
regard, the recent enactment by Congress of the "Law to Modernize the State" provides an
institutional framework that eliminates the unnecessary controls and regulations that served as
obstacles for the normal conduct of business, while at the same time allowing for a reduction
of the size of government to a size consistent with the modem concept of an efficient and
expeditious administration.
With the adoption of these policies our Country will be able to successfully compete
in the regional markets that might result as a consequence of the efforts that we are making
together with other Governments in the area.
2.    Geneal Transport Policy
Government policy for the transport sector envisages the participation of the private
sector in the administration of those services in which it has shown a greater efficiency and



- 43 -                           ANNEL1
Page 2 of 11
effectiveness. This policy will in turn reflect itself in the downsizing of the government with
the corresponding reduction in public expenditure.
With regards to the construction, rehabilitation and maintenance of our road system,
private sector participation is already a reality. In fact, we feel certain that we will meet our
target for 1993, which requires that at least 70% of all road maintenance be carried out by
private contractors.
At present we are in the process of preparing several bills to be submitted to Congress
to restructure the ports and airports subsectors, as well as to modernize our Transport Law.
These bills will include the transfer of certain activities to the private sector in an orderly,
transparent and competitive fashion. Purthermore, in the case of the Transport Law, the
review will include our intention to foster unimpeded access and competition. Finally, the
Government will review the mechanism presently in place to subsidize public transportation in
the cities to ensure that it benefits only those who need it, while avoiding waste and abuses.
The Ministry of Communications, Public Works and Transport, SECOPT, has already
begun the preparation of studies for the control of vehicle weights and for the improvement of
road maintenance. The Ministry also routinely carries out preinvestment studies, which
include the economic and environmental evaluation and subsequently the design of the selectea
projects. This process provides the basis for the preparation of the Ministry's investment
program.
The, continuity of the construction, rehabilitation and maintenance of our road system
is provided by the flnancing that we obtain from multilateral organizations and friendly
governments for a time-slice of the investment program. The selection of the works is based
partly on the results of a planning model used by the Ministry, which analyzes the different
options in a national context and which makes it possible to reflect the changes and
innovations that occur in the sector. This results in an optimal use of the funds available.
3.    Legal, Iastitutional and Regulatory Framework
In order to make it possible for SECOPT to fully discharge its duties, the Government
has created a new office of Under Secretary of Planning and Finance. This will give the
sector planning area a level and prominence consistent with the important role that it must
play. Similarly, in order to better study and protect the environment, a new Environmental
Unit has been created as part of the Department of Roads. This new unit will ensure that the
possible negative impact that the works built by SECOPT, and other government agencies that
work in the sector, might have on the environment is avoided or mitigated.
4.    The Road Subsector
Despite the important investments made in our road system, much work remains to be
done in order to make it an instrument that fully supports the development of the country and
the central american integration process. In order to preserve the investmnits in the sector,
the Government has decided to rehabilitate the roads that had deteriorated and to establish a
maintenance system that prevents further deterioration.



Page 3 of 11
The sectors to be rehabilitated were chosen by means of a ranking process that
considers the subsector as whole and that includes all the works identified as part of the Road
Master Plan prepared in 1989. The analysis used the model HDM m, which has been
adopted by SECOPT as its basic planning tool. This model was also used to update the main
and secondary road Master Plan in May 1991, and the access roads Master Plan in July 1992.
Finally, SECOPT will engage consultants to assist in the review of its standards for bridge
and road design, including measures to protect the environment.
As previously mentioned, the Government has begun to restructure and rationalize the
activities of SECOPT so that those activities that can be carried out more efficiently by the
private sector are transferred to it. To implement this policy. SECOPT has begun to hire
private contractors to maintain the road network, and it is estimated that in 1993 70% of all
maintenance work will be carried out by contractors. This measure has resulted in the
reduction of the staff of the Directorate General for Road Maintenance, from 5,000 employees
in 1989 to 3,600 in May 1991, and 2,350 by end of 1992. It is estimated that by 1994 the
number of employees will be around 1,700. It should be mentioned that this significant
reduction has been accomplished without producing more unemployment siice SECOPT
managed to transfer most of its former employees to the private contractors, acting at times as
placement agency. This solicitous attitude allowed SECOPT to successfully manage a
situation that could otherwise have been unmanageable.
The policies implemented by SECOPT have resulted in marked growth of small
private contractors, as well as of very small and even individual contractors. Similarly,
efficiency has almost doubled, considering the lower costs and shorter completion times of the
contracted works. Furthermore, the reduction of the participation of SECOPT in maintenance
led to considerable amount of idle equipment in the Ministry, which has been transferred to
the private sector through public auctions. This has in turn resulted in the expansion of the
private contractors, which has led to greater competition, lower prices and increased capacity
to undertake larger works. SECOPT has also improved the utilization of its asphalt plants,
increasing the production from 15 MT per day to 800 MT per day. After covering its own
needs, SECOPT sells the rest to private contractors and other Government agencies, which
makes the operesion of these plants a viable and economic proposition.
As part of its policy to improve and assure the proper maintenance of its road system,
the Government has decided to set up a Road Fund. To this end, SECOPT is in the process
of selecting the consulting firm that will study the different options available, including
contributions from the users, as well as the provision of funds by other agencies. The study
will recommend the best alternative and propose a plan for its implementation, including the
definition of the operating systems and the required legislation. We estimate that this study
will be completed in mid 1993 and that the Fund could be created in 1994.
To protect the investment made in the road system, SECOPT will also prepare, with
the assistance of consultants, a study to improve matnance planning, including the control
of vehicle weight. Finally, to expand, rehabilitate and maintain the road network, SECOPT
has prepared an investment program for the 1992-1996 period, which incorporates a gradual
increase in the funds allocated to main ce up to approximately US$45 million in 1996.



45-                                   I
Page 4 0f 11
5.    Ports Subsector
Consistent with the Goverment's policy to increase efficiency in the economy
through fostering a greater participation of the private sector in those areas where it has a
comparative advantage, the Board of Directors of the National Ports Authority, ENP,
approved in June 1992 an Action Plan that defines the role to be played by the public and
private sectors in the operation of our ports. The Action Plan also provides a framework for
decision making and policy definition in this very important area of our economy. ENP also
set up a Technical Unit so that, together with consultants that will soon be hired, it can
prepare studies to improve the efficiency of the operation of our ports through the
participation of the private sector. We andcipate that this can be accomplished through the
contracting of the operation and/or lease of zones, areas and/or port services, as well as
through the creation of an administratively and operationally independent unit in ENP to
manage the container terminal in Puerto Cortes.
In order to expedite and supervise the implementation of the Action Plan, the
Government created, by Decree 001945 dated September 16, 1992, a Committee made up of
representatives of SECOPT, the Ministry of Economy and Commerce and the Ministry of
Finance and Public Credit. The Committee has been supervising the process and it will be an
important instrument to ensure the implementation of the Plan.
Beside completing the definition of policies to modernize the port subsector and
leading the transformation of the subsector, the Committee (assisted by coasultants to be hired
under the Credit that we are requesting from IDA) will also have the following important
responsibilities: (a) study the operations of the port in La Ceiba, considering inter ali the
transfer of the cargo operations to the Customs Office and the docking facilities to the
Chamber of Commerce or the Municipality; (b) take steps to ensure that a tariff study is
prepared based on the costs of operating the ports, with emphasis on Puerto Cortes; (c) defne
the participation of tLt private sector in the financing required to rehabilitate the dock at Tela
and determine the mechanisms and contracts required for the efficient operation of that port;
(d) ensure that the operation of the ports in Puerto Cortes, Castilla and San Lorenzo is studied
so as to identify those services that can be provided more efficiently by the private sector, and
(e) make a recommendation as to the role to be played by the National Port Authority in the
restructured port subsector.
Once the results of the above mentioned studies are available, the Government will
implemens all those measures that are consistent with its policy to improve the efficiency of
the port subsector. Finally, in that which concerns tariffs, the Government will ensure,
through its representatives in the Public Services Supervisory Commission, CNSSP, that the
Commission radfies that the tariffs that it authorizes are upper limits, and that the regulated
agencies are free to charge different tariffs provided that they do not exceed those authorized
by the Commission.
6.    Airport Subsector
Our Civil Aeronautics Law, although substantially correct and usefil, needs to be
updated in order to keep pace with the technological advances and the rapid progress of civil
aviation. Furthermore, the rather limited budget of the Ditmorate General of Civil
Aeronautics makes it impossible for it to provide an efficient and adequate airport service.
Finally, the international conventions and agreements signed by Honduras must be refected in



- 46 -                           AN X 1
Page 5 of 11
our legal system in order to more easily comply with the requirements of modern civil
aviation.
In order to increase the efficiency of the subsector, the Government has relied on the
advice provided by experts of the ICAO financed by UNDP. These experts have made
several important suggestions that have allowed us to begin improving the performance of the
subsector. Additionally, we expect to retain the services of other experts to provide assistance
in economics, planning and in the preparation of a Master Plan. Finally, the Government
intends to hire five experts to professionally run the Tegucigalpa and San Pedro Sula airports;
of these, two will be Hondurans in order to ensure the success of the program even after the
departure of the international experts. On the basis of plans prepared by these experts, the
Government will budget the amounts needed to properly operate and maintain our existing
facilities and we foresee that in three or four years the budgeted amounts will be compatible
with the income directly produced by the airports.
With regards to new investments, the Government will undertake only those that have
been shown to be economically feasible. Similarly, on the basis of the, Master Plan to be
prepared with the assistance of consultants, the Government will select the most efficient and
effective manner to manage the airports, including operation contracts with the private sector,
as well on the hiring of certain services or areas with private contractors.
7.    Railroad Subsector
In this subsector the Government intends to transfer to the private sector, and when
this proves not to be possible close down, all those activities for which service is presently
being provided by others and that cannot be run profltably by the Railways Authority, FNH.
In this manner we will increase the efficiency of the subsector and reduce the budget deflcit
without affecting the users, since there will always be several private contractors to continue
with the provision of equivalent services. SECOPT will use its experience to assist FNH to
help the personnel that must be laid off to find new employment.
Mauro Membreflo Tosta
Ministry of SECOPT
(Letter signed, December 23, 1992)
