HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPNT PLAN
A. INTRODUCTION
1.     Based on the institutional analysis carried out during project preparation, the Government
has agreed to an Institutional Development Plan to strengthen SECOPT's transport planning and
environmental protection function. As a first step, the Government created the Under Secretariat of
Planning and Finance (SSPF). Appointment of the Vice-Minister of SSPF and of at least one
economist and one engineer each would be a condition of credit effectiveness. This would be the
core group in charge of implementing the Institutional Development Plan. The plan includes:
Clarification of the objectives of SSPF and DGT.
A restatement of:
The responsibilities of the SSPF.
. SSPF structure in relation with its corresponding
responsibilities.
A redefinition of the role of DGT.
Identification and implementation of the organizadon and operating capacity (number
of technicians and professional profile) that would be required to perform the
referenced responsibilities appropriately. Design and implementation of a staffing
plan.
Definition of procedures and basic mechanisms for implementation of the planning,
programming and budgeting process.
A training and technical assistance program geared toward the development of the
necessary technical capability to carry out planning, programming, budgeting,
monitoring and environmental protection, as well as inter-institutional coordination
with respect to environmental concerns in SECOFP.
B. OBJECTVE OF TH   NSTITVTIONAL DEVELOPMENT PLAN
2.     The objective of the Institutional Development Plan is to strengthen SSPF capabilities to
carry out its planning and coordinating responsibilities, including the incorporation of environmental
concerns (Atachment I of this amnex provides further details of SSPF and the environmental unit
fiucdons). The plan would, in particular:



- 54 -
ANNEX 
Page 2 of 8
(1)    Ensure that SECOPT's activities are within the framework of a national transport
sector development policy.
(2)    Ensure that sector analysis and decision-making capacity is strengthened through
development of a transport information network and capacity for analysis of impact
of macro-economic and sector policies.
(3)   Integrate the different departments within the Secretariat into a unified approach and
a single planning, programming, budgeting and monitoring system, allowing greater
linkage of organizational plans with sector policies, directives, and budgets.
(4)   Ensure that projects and works executed by the Secretariat are based on sound
feasibility assessment of technical, economic and environmental factors.
C. RESOURCES NEEDED TO EMPLEM4NT lE INSTITUIONAL DEVELOPMNT PLAN
3.     Achievement of the above objectives would require adequate strengthening of SSPF through
appropriate staffing and investments in technical assistance, training and equipment. These inputs
would be targeted on:
(1)   revising organizational and inter-organizational structures, functions and procedures,
including increasing levels of decentralization, as appropriate;
(2)   developing staff skills in priority areas; and
(3)   designing and implementing new technical support systems for transport information,
and financial and environmental management.
D.     SSPF ORGANIZATION AND SECTORIAL PLANNING COORDINATION
4.     To carry out its planning, programming, budgeting and monitoring responsibilities, SECOPT
has proposed a new structure which, besides the decree for the creation of SSPF incorporates a
"Planning Committee". This Committee will aim to articulate and coordinate actions among
SECOPT's technical-administrative units, and between the entities involved in planning and sectorial
development under the Secretariat's responsibility (Graph 1 shows the organizational relations in
planning and monitoring within SECOPT). This coordinating effort is particularly important since
SSPF will incorporate to its new structure a new environmental department, the HDM model, and
transport information systems working across different organizational structures. Sitting members at
the committee would be SECOPT's Vice-ministers and directors, acting as executive secretary the
Vice-Minister of SSPF. This committee's main functions would be to:
(1)   Establish and/or strengthen mechanisms for cooperation and collaboration among
SECOPT and other sector organizations;



- 55 -
ANNE 2
Page 3 of 8
(2)    Evaluate and discuss basic sector studies, including analysis of impact of economic
and sector policies;
(3)    Review the consolidated programming of SECOPT and take concerted actions in
accordance to priorities and availability of funds;
(4)    Exchange information, through periodic reports issued by its technical secretariat, on
the progress of physical execution and make recommendations for readjustments or
reorientation.
5.     To carry out the expected SSPF functions, adequate staffing would be required. Not later
than October 31, 1993, during the first phase of the technical assistance program (see para. 7(b)),
SSPF will prepare a staffing plan which will be implemented during the second phase of the Program
(para. 7(c)). For the environmental unit, staffing would include: (i) a civil engineer with experience
in environmental protection and natural resources management, and with at least five years experience
in civil works and construction, and (ii) two civil engineers with training in one of the following
subjects: biology/ecology, edafology, hydrology and economy. Satisfactory staffing of the
environmental unit is a condition of credit effectiveness. Further determination of structure, functions
and staff, would be defined during the implementation of the first phase of the project technical
assistance which is described in the following section.
F. TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE AND TRAINING PROGRAM
Objectives:
(a)    To contribute to overcome the deficits of individual staff skills in transport planning
and environmental protection detected in the institutional analysis carried out during
project preparation; and
(b)    To generate and transfer to the referred staff, the necessary skills to implement
planning, monitoring and evaluation management, including the environmental
factor.
Approach:
6.     The approach to technical assistance and training for institutional development would include
the following key elements:
(a)    Participatory methods would be used for the diagnosis. design and implementation of
improved systems an procedures, including the conduct of both inter and intra-
organizational action-planning workshops, as necessary. These workshops would
also provide a forum to help identify the staffing needs of SSPF. These workshops
will bring together key working level and senior staff and expert consultants to
address major areas of sector planning improvements. Working groups would be
used to follow up the results of the workshops in order to further develop, refine and
implement the outlined plans;



-56 -
ANNEX 2
Page 4 of 8
(b)    Technical assistance would be conducted primarily in a collaborative mode,
developing iternal capacit by assistig in the work of the planning staff as they
join SSPF, rather then repacing those staff by directly carrying out their functions.
In specific technical assistance areas, involving one time only functions, consulting
assistance would not be directly tied to capacity building of sector staff (e.g. systems
programming for the sector information network or for a computerized chart of
accounts);
(c)    Action-training would be used to achieve specific skill development in priority areas.
In action-training, trainees learn skills relevant to their immediate work needs by
addressing real work problems in training sessions; and
(d)    The design of the institutional development effort would be linked to annual project
planning workshops, in which issues are discussed, priorities established, and
integration achieved among institutional development and other sector activities.
(e)    Technical assistance will be implemented on a two phased sequence. During the
first phase a small expatriate team (para 7(b)) will assist SECOPT in defining the
organization structure and functions of the Planning System, including the role that
DGT should have in the process, as well as defining the type of technical assistance
and training to be implemented during the second phase.
7.     Because of the intensity of the institutional development activities supported by this project,
fSequent supervision by IDA staff, including staff with skill and knowledge in institutional
development, would be essential.
Ioslitutlonal Development:
(a)    Government Actions and Decisions. As part of project launching efforts, SSPF and
key SECOPT directorates (DGC, DGCCA, DGT, etc.) would be assembled to work
in both phases of the institutional development program. They would not only be
instrumental in establishing the new planning system but their staff would also be
subject to intensive training programs.
(b)    Phase 1: Institutional and Work Team Planning. (8 man-months). In cooperation
with SSPF counterpart group, consisting of the Vice-Minister and a core of
professionals, a technical assistance team composed by a consultant on Institutional
Development, a Consultant on Formulation of Monitoring and Evaluation Systems, a
Consultant on Environmental Protection and Conservation of Natural Resources, and
an Information Systems Consultant would undertake structured, organizational
planning workshops for a period of about two months. These workshops would
involve SECOPT's planning and environmental management staff to clarify and
develop a common understanding of: a) SSPF and DGT overall missions, policies
and objectives, as well as the best organizational structure to discharge its
responsibilities regarding environ l issues, information systems, monitoring,
project formulation and administration; b) the roles, responsibilities and inter-
relationships of units within SECOPT and autonomous agencies under the



-57 -
ANNEX 2
Page 5of 8
Secretariat's authority; c) an outline of a strategic plan, for phase 2 discussed below,
for organizational activities and for the use of project inputs (i.e., technical
assistance, training awd equipment). The strategic plan would include, inter alia: (i)
firm-up training needs which during project preparation were identified in the areas
of: Formulation of Sectorial Development Policies; Formulation of Transportation
Policies and Strategies; Project Formulation and Evaluation; Environmental Factors
in Project Formulation; Budgetary Planning; Formulation of Planning aud
Monitoring Systems; Formulation and Administration of Regional and International
Projects; Management Information Systems in Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation;
Report Preparation at the Management Level; (ii) define specific training targ-ts (a
preliminary identification is presented in Table 1), and (iii) elaborate technical
assistance and training terms of reference, as well as estimating Phase 2
implementation resources.
(c)    Phase 2. Institutional Reform and Training. (112 man-months). Given the
foundation provided by Phase 1, the emphasis in Phase 2 would be on
implementation. This is when the program to strengthen SECOPT's planning and
coordinating capabilities through SSPF and DGT would be implemented and
* environmental management and information systens development would be done.
Progress would be reviewed during annual Project Review and Planning Workshops,
and plans revised in a timely fashion to address any changing conditions and
technical requirements.
8.     Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation. A key element of planning processes would be the
participation of the different technical-administrative units of the Secretariat and of other autonomous
entities involved in planning and sectorial development which fall under SECOPT's responsibility.
Special attention will be dedicated to the organization and operation of the new Planning
9.     In accordance with its role as the Committee's Executive Secretariat (and institutional
coordinator), the SSPF planning, programming and monitoring functions must be strengthened
appropriate technical levels.
10.    Planning and evaluation procedures, should also be established and implemented in the form
of manuals, procedural guidelines, and/or regulations not only to assist internal operations but also
allow consistent inter-institutional coordination and processes.
11.    Emphasis must be placed on staffing and training SSPF personnel on all aspects around
budget analysis and its management vis a vis planning and investment requirements.
12.    Environmental Protection. The creation of a specialized unit able to deal with
environmental issues is an indication of increasing Government concern in the field. Given the level
of complexity of most of the environmental problems facing each one of SECOPT's Directorates, a
study carried-out during project preparation, recommended the strengthening of a small existing
environmental unit, its transformation into a SSPF Department and the definition of its terms of
reference.



- 58 -
ANNEX2
Page 6 of 8
13.    A basic core staff, which must be hired and in place by the initiation of the project, would
work with an expatriate technical assistance team.
14.    A clear definition of objectives, functions, and internal organization, as well as internal
manuals and operational regulations, would be part of this institutional building effort. This wIll be
accompanied by clear definition of service relationships with the DOPS and other institutions as well
as its operational strategy.
15.    Personnel training, in its different forms, and the creation and operation of an appropriate
information system, must constitute a central focus of the department's consolidation, during project
execution and beyond.
16.    Sector Budgeting and Programming. The budgeting and financial management function
parallels and must be integrated with SSPF planning function. SSPF would be responsible for
disseminating budgetary guidance, within the Minister's "Frame of Orientation", specially as
Executive Secretariat of the Planning Committee and responsible for analyzing proposed budgets and
actual expenditures of sector organizations as input to the Minister's review and decision making
process.
17.    These functions include, but are not limited to, evaluating and reporting on actual program
expenditures within the sector, and providing guidance to SECOPT's Directorates and sector
organizations on the development of organizational budget and accounting systems so that they are
consistent across the sector and meet sector planning requirements.
18.    Information Systems, Collection and Analysis. This component within SSPF and DGT
would support the implementation of functions like:
(a)    Assisting other units within SSPF to meet data collection, storage, and processing
needs (including the use of planning, GIS, and statistical packages and required
programming) through: i) the development of a sector management and information
network that integrates the data collection and processing activities of sector
organization; and ii) the development and maintenance of SSPF and DGT data bases
and processing hardware and software; and
(b)    Establishing technical standards for the development of information systems within
the sector that would permit the necessary links among SECOPT's offices and
Directorates and between sector organizations. The use of an information task force
under the Sector Planning Committee would assure coordination and consistency in
the use of information linkages.
19.    Summary of Institutional Development Inputs. Technical assistance, training and
procurement of equipment in support of the development of the described two phased approach,
would be financed by the project. Financing would be provided to assist SSPF and DGT and other
sector organizations to design and install systems, strengthen operations, train staff, and conduct
analytical work. With reference to technical assistance, it should be noted that an impartant principle
would be to clearly differentiate among specific consulting roles in the terms of reference. This



- 59 -
AN,NE 2
Page 7 of 8
would occur during Phase 1 in which a detailed analysis on organization, structure, roles and
functions would allow a precise identification of needed expertise. It must also be recognized dth
institutional development and production of specific products sometimes require alternative approaches
by technical assistance personnel.

Responsibilities of SECOPr's Under-Secretariat of Planning and Finance
.    'The Institutional Development Plan would help to develop the necessary procedures and
provide technical assistance and training to SSPF to effectively assume the following responsibilities:
General Responsibilities
Policy:
(a)    Develop policies for the approval of the Secretary to implement a sectorial planning
and environmental protection system within the scope of the Secretariat's
responsibility;
Advia=r:
(b) Advis the Secretary and Assistant Secretaries and thie various Secretariat Departments
on Planning and Programming, Monitoring and Evaluation, Environmental viability,
inter-institutional Coordination, tapping of resources and other areas within the scope
of its responsibility;
(c)    Assist the Secretary and Assistant Secretaries before International and Integration
Commissions and Organizations in order to represent them or obtain technical or
financial assistance;
(d)    Coordina with the corresponding agencies in charge of national planning, the
development of SECOPT's sectorial policy;
(e)    Coordinate with the Coordination and Budget Secretariat (SECPLAN), the
development of medium- and long-term execution and investment plans ithin the
scope of the Secretariat's responsibilities;
(f)    Coordinate with autonomous and other national, regional and international entities
working in related SECOPT's activities, with respect to Planning, Monitoring,
Evaluation and Environmental Protection;
(g) Coordinate with national, regional and international entities with a view to entering
into Agreements - Loan, Technical Assistance, Participation Agreements and others;
monitor their progress and compliance with agreements;
(h)    Codi   actions with the Assistant Secretaries to channel and tap technical,
financial and cooperation resources to support the execution of programs;

(i)    Develop SECOPT directives and technical and budeetary procedures for planning
programs and projects;
0)    Develop directives, regulations and procedures to include environmental analysis as
part of the technical and eponomic analysis in the design of programs and projects;
(k)    Establish internal mechanisms and procedures in the Secretariat to ensure coordination
and integration among its different units with respect to Planning, Monitoring and
Evaluation;
(1)    Participate in *he modernization and computerization of the Secretariat's administrative
processes;
Planning:

with participation from the different departments within the Secretariat;
(n)    Develop training, technical assistance and cooperation programs within the scope of
responsibility of the Planning Directorate in SSPF and execute the same in
coordination with the different departments within the Secretariat;
Monitorin :
(o)    Establish and implement a monito; ing system of the physical and financial progress of
SECOPT's programs and projects;
(p)    Monitor, on a permanent basis, the Secretariat's different departments to ensure
implementation of the various established directives, regulations and procedures in the
planning aad environmental protection areas;
Evaluation:
(q)    Establish and implement a project/program impact evaluation system including as one

(r)    Perform technical, economic and environmental impact evaluations of the projects and

program and implement it;
Information:
(u)    Prepare periodic and special reports on the progress of programs and projects and
submit them to the corresponding authorities;
(v)    Assume responsibility for the correct functioning of SECOPT's Computer Center,
proceeding with automation of the main systems of interest to the institution;
(w)   Manage a computerized data bank to systematize and provide information on the
Secretariat's general and specific statistics for decision-making, information on
subjects of specific technical interest, specialized bibliography, and others;
(x)    All other responsibilities delegated by the Secretary within the scope of its
responsibility.
Ploaming, Studies and Coordination
(a)    Advise the Planning Director on all areas related with coordination for the
development of SECOPT's sectorial policy;
(b)    Advise the Planning Director on preparation of medium and long term execution and

(c)    Advise the Planning Director on mobilizing technical, financial and cooperation
resources;
(d)    Advise the Director on his dealings with Commissions and international and
integration organizations;
(e)    Advise the Director on his dealings with respect to Loan, Technical Cooperation and
Participation Agreements, and others;
(f)    Develop, in coordination with the Monitoring and Environmental Protection
Departments, the directives and technical and budgetary procedures for planning the
Secretariat's plans, programs and projects;

(g)   Prepare directives, regulations and procedures for the incorporation of technical and

(h)   Develop an action programming and formulation of feasibility projects system that

(i)    Support the Secretariat's departments in the implementation of directives, regulations

0)    Coordinate with the different SECOPT departments, the Secretariat's annual technical

(k)    Determine the needs for studies, consultants, technical and cooperation assistance in

(im)   Develop and provide information and analysis for decision-making on the

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
THE ROAD SUBSECTOR
The National Road Network
1.    The national road network of Honduras consists of 14,100 km of at. weather roads of which
2400 km are paved and 11,700 km unpaved (Table 1). Functionaily, the network is classified into
Primary roads (3033 km), Secondary roads (2424 kin) and Feeder roads (8619 kIn).
2.    Except for the northeast part of the country (the Province of "Gracias a Dios") where few
roads exist (246 km), the remainder of the country is reasonably well served by the national road
network. In terms of importance, the Northern Highway which connects Tegucigalpa with San Pedro
Sula (the second largest city) and Puerto Cortes (Honduras' main port) is the country's road
backbone. Also important are the Southern Highway and the Pan American Highway. The former
serves the southern region of the country including the port of San Lorenzo and the latter links
Honduras with El Salvador and Nicaragua. The north coast (Tela, La Ceiba, Trujillo) can easily be
reached via San Pedro Sula/El Progreso and the east and southeast regions, via Tegucigalpa. The
Western Highway, which begins at Chamelecon in the north, serves the northwest part of the country
while the road from Siguatepeque via La Esperanza and Gracias, can be used to access this area from
Tegucigalpa.
3.    The Pan American Highway and the Western Highway are the main corridors running through
Central America. However, only the former highway has consistent design standards on both sides of
the border. The roads from Guatemala and El Salvador connecting to the Western Highway are
unpaved and in poor condition. A regional studyl! examined the various land corridors to strengthen
the integration of Central America. Both these roads were included in the study with the former
being on the "natural" corridor while the latter, which runs through Nueva Ocotepeque - La Entrada -
Chamelecon - Tegucigalpa - Danli - Las Manos, is considered to be a good alternative.
4.    Inadequate maintenance levels, weak institutional capabilities, and relaxation of axle load
regulations, led to the deterioration of the road network. At the end of 1990, about 27% of the paved
roads were in good condition, compared to 61% in 1985. The current administration, however,
expects that the paved network will be fully rehabilitated by the end of 1996.
I/ Study fnanced by UNDP at the regp  of the Thnort Ministers of the Cenrl Ameiican counies.

Highway Traffic and Veide Fleet
5.    Traffic volume information is scattered as there is no traffic data collection program in place.
With the information available, it was possible to establish the major flow patterns in the country.
The major flows are concentrated on the primary road network particularly along the Northern
Highway and the road between San Pedro Sula and El Progreso. Some 4500 vehicles per day (vpd)
use the Northrn Highway (Table 2) with freight vehicles (trucks and pick-ups), making up about
30% of the traffic. The two-lane road section between San Pedro Sula and the airport (11 km),
carries 12,000 vpd. From the airport to El Progreso (16 km), traffic levels drop to 6500 vpd.
Traffic volumes on other main roads radiating from Tegucigalpa, are estimated at about 2200 vpd.
6.    Honduras' vehicle fleet increased from approximately 29,000 vehicles in 1970 to an estimated
155,000 vehicles in 1990 (Table 3) with the number of trucks. utility vehicles and buses increasing
more rapidly than automobiles. Vehicle motorization, in terms of automobiles per 1000 inhabitants,
increased over the same period from 5 to approximately 11.
Road Safely
7.    Road safety is under the responsibility of the Police Department. One of the main causes of
the accidents that occur on the highway network is the lack of adequate vertical signalization,
pavement markings and safety barriers. SECOPT is developing, with IDB assistance, a country-wide
program to improve the situation and decrease the number of road accidents. The road rehabilitation
program to be partly financed by x. `3 is also addressing this issue at the project level by including
funds for vertical signs and road markings.
Institdonal Framework
8.    The Secretariat of Public Works and Transport (SECOPT) directly (through its various
directorates) or indirectly (through its public companies), has overall responsibility for planning,
coordination, and regulation of activities in Honduras' transport sector. For highways, there are two
general directorates, namely Roads (DUC)  and Maintenan  (DGCCA) which report to the Deputy
Minister of Public Works. These directorates operate under separate budgets. DGC is responsible
for the planning and emxecution of road construction, reconstruction and rehabilitation projects and
DGCCA for road (and airport) maintenance.
9.    The overall organizational structure of DGC has remained virtually unchanged over the last
few years. Notwithstanding a 22% vat in personnel during 1990 (1800 persons: 200 technical and
1600 field), DGC seems to respond quite adequately to the demands of SECOPT. Projects with
foreign financing are the responsibility of three separate project implementation units (Unidades
Ejecutoras), ruamely: CABEI-FIV which handles projects financed by the Central American Bank for
Economic Integration (CABEI) and by the Venezuelan Investment Fund (FIV); IDB-BIRF which
looks after the Interamerican Bank and World Bank projects; and AID-PRODESBA which is
responsible for projects with AID and PRODESBA/IDA financing. Local consultants and/or
consulting firms are retained to aid in road design and construction supervision activities.



- 70 -
ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 7
10.   The DGCCA, on the other hand, is undergoing major changes. In terms of personnel, the
target is to reach a staff of only 1700 by the end of 1994 (down from 5000 in 1989). To compensate
for this, DGCCA intends to contract about two thirds of the road maintenance work with the private
sector, with the remaining one third to be done by force account. This in-house maintenance capacity
will also provide backup for emergencies and reduce the risk of replacing a public monopoly by a
cartel of private interests. In terms of its organization, DGCCA has made significant changes both at
the central office as well as at the district level. Th%se changes are aimed at reducing the size of the
organization and streamlining maintenance operations. DGCCA is setting up project implementation
units for its maintenance operations, particularly those under contract.
11.   Generally speaking, the technical quality of DGC's and DGCCA's professional staff, is
satisfactory. However, the quality of engineering studies carried out by consultants are often
deficient. This is in part due to the lack of comprehensive engineering standards and technical
specifications for road construction applicable to the local conditions and requirements. SECOPT,
with IDB assistance, will address this issue through the preparation of road technical specifications
and of road geometric design standards!. Another subject that needs special attention is the question
of slope stability problems found in mountainous road sections. Some of the primary roads
particularly the Western Highway are affected by slope stability problems which increase the cost of
road rehabilitation and reconstruction. Again, IDB has included a technical assistance component to
address this issue3'.
Road Maintenance
12.   Road maintenance is the responsibility of DGCCA and its 13 districts, with four districts,
Tegucigalpa, Galeras, San Pedro Sula, and Santa Rosa de Copan, being responsible for nearly 50% of
the network. With the reduction in staff and the increasing privatization of maintenance operations
districts are assuming more of a supervisory role. The maintenance by contract effort is showing
positive results; at the end of the first year of operation, SECOPT estimated that maintenance by
contract to be more cost-effective than force account, with savings in the order of 40%. DGCCA is
considering, as part of the maintenance privatization actions, the awarding of pluriannual contracts for
routine and periodic maintenance for all road types within a sub-region of the country. This scheme
could include the use of small enterprises (micro-empresas), under a cooperative system to carry out
routine maintenance work. The small enterprises scheme has been successfully implemented in
Colombia. Typically, microenterprises would be made up of 10 to 14 men, preferably living in the
surrounding area, who carry out the manual routine maintenance on approximately 50 km of road
under contract with SECOPT. Contracts would be limited to one year (renewable). Under this
arrangement, small enterprises provide their own tools but, when required, mechanisms could be
established for SECOPI to provide financial assistance for the purchase of tools which the micro-
enterprises pay back through deductions from their monthly payment. This maintenance alternative
can also be used to encourage SECOPT employees to resign from their jobs to take on a road
maintenance contract. High level staff from DGCCA visited Colombia in mid-1991 to become
acquainted with the concept.

13.   With increasing maintenance work done by the private sector, it will be easier to link the
allocation of funds to explicit physical output (accountability). The existing system to audit district
performance is inadequate as it does not readily permit a comparison between funds spent and actual
work performed. This issue will be addressed under the IDB financed technical assistance to
strengthen DGC and DGCCA in contract management and performance budgets.
Road Maintenance Equipment and Workshops
14.   Concurrently with staff reductions and privatization of maintenance operations, DGCCA is
reducing its mamntenance equipment fleet from the current 11 16 units to 540. Forty three percent of
the equipment is non-operational and of this 10-15% is scrap. DGCCA is in the process of appraising
and auctioning the existing equipment to private contractors. One such auction was carried out very
successfully in early 1992 with the final selling price, on the average, well above the base price.
This, to some extent, was caused by the demand for construction equipment on the part of the
contractors bidding for on-going road works. Tied to the question of the equipment fleet is the future
of the regional workshops located in Tegucigalpa and San Pedro Sula, and of the crushing and asphalt
plants, four of each kind, which are located at various sites.- The regional shops were created to
take care of major repairs to SECOPT equipment leaving lighter repairs to the each of the thirteen
districts. This concept has not worked satisfactorily; shops are overstaffed and their productivity is
very low due in part to the unavailability of spare parts and the lack of specialized tools required to
work on heavy equipment. As a general rule, a piece of equipment entering the workshops for repair
would not be in working order for several months. Most of the delays are caused either by the time
it takes to order and receive the required spare part, or by the fact that every new entry would be
stripped of its usable parts to facilitate the repair of a prior arrival, thus, when the requested spare
part arrives, the equipment unit is in need of other parts. As a result of this practice (to some extent
unavoidable given the inefficient procurement process), most of the workshop space is taken up by
dismantded equipment in search for usable parts. DGCCA is currently streamlining the procurement
procedures, in general, and studying a number of options with regards to the future of these shops
including the participation of the private sector. Also, the assistance to be provided by the IDB will
address these issues.
Axle Weight Control
15. The Transport Directorate (DGT) of SECOPT is responsible for enforcing axle load
regulations as well as vehicle dimensions and overall weight restrictions. Axle load regulations were
enacted in 1976 (rable 4). The axle limits set forth in the 1976 legislation for the most common axle
arrangements are: 8 tons for single axles with dual wheels; 14.5 tons for tandem axles with dual
wheels; and 18.5 tons for triple axles with dual wheels. Also the legislation establishes maximum
weights for each truck configuration; these maximum legal weights range from 12 tons for two axle
trucks to 49 tons for five axle articulated trucks. There are 15 axle load control stations at various
locations on the primary road network which record the vehicle type and weight and other valuable
/ An,sbalt plants are located at: El Palrar near San Pedro Sul, (2 plants), Nacaome in the south, and Rio
oiadAo on the road Tegucigalpa-Cataam. Cnuhing plants are located at: El Palmar, Ojo de Agua on the
road betwoen Togucigalpa and Danli, and Rio Hondo and Guayave on the road to Cata_m.

ANNEX 3
Page 5 of 7
information on the type of merchandise being transported and its origin and destination. In 1986, the
regulations were relaxed to permit truckers to carry an additional 15% load. This measure increased
the overall permissible weight for articulated trucks by 4 to 5 tons, depending on the vehicle size. No
studies are available on the impact of this measure but the current administration is going back to the
1976 legislation. The IDB program will examine this issue and develop and implement a system of
axle-load controls, which includes the procurement of related equipment.
Design and Implementation of a HIighway Mnagement System
16.   To enable SECOPT to prepare systematic pluri-annual road investment and performance plans,
organize maintenance activities particularly maintenance by contract, monitor program implementation
and equipment management system and train SECOPT personnel, the efforts undertaken to implement
the Bank's Highway Design and Maintenance Standards Model, Version HI (HDM-l) is being
supplemented by the establishment of a highway management system under the IDB loan.
Highway Expenditues
17.   Given the deterioration of the network in the last few years, it can be concluded that there has
been an inefficient use of limited road maintenance resources. Also, the lack of an adequate auditing
system and of sufficient and appropriate equipment, have contributed to this process. To ensure the
road network receives the attention it needs, the Government should guarantee a continuous and
timely flow of funds to provide adequate maintenance levels. The present administration has shown a
deot nite commitment to rehabilitate and properly maintain the present infrastructure and has made
additional funds available for road maintenance activities.
IF.   The table below shows the expenditures on road construction and rehabilitation (DGC) and
road maintenance (DGCCA), for the period 1988-1991. As no earmarking exists (with the exception
of the tolls being collected at San Pedro Sula), road works are funded through central government
budget appropriations and external sources. During 1990 and 1991, DGCCA's expenditures
correspond to the rehabilitation works currently underway on the primary road network (Northern
Highway, Southern Highway etc.). DGCCA's 1991 expenditures, which more than doubled the 1990
levels, were channeled to remove the existing maintnance backlog. As road rehabilitation tapers off
in four to five years, maintenance financing is expected to increase even fuiher. Prior to 1990,
nearly 70% of the road maintenance salaries were paid to personnel not involved in maintenance
operations (administrative personnel). The staff reduction measures underway in DGCCA are
addressing this issue. Out of the 1000 employees laid off during 1990, 90% were from the above
group diminishing considerably the percentage of non-productive staff. Since January 1991, an
additional 400 employees were let go and again a large proportion of them came from the same
group.

19.   By 1996, when the current rehabilitation effort is completed, the annual maintenance
expenditures needed to keep the paved road network in good condition and to provide a minimum
level of maintenance to the remaining network, will amount to approximately US$45 million. One
third of this amount will be spent on routine maintenance and two thirds on periodic maintenance. To
reach that expenditure level, the Government is planning to increase the maintenance budget as
follows: US$35 in 1993; US$37 million in 1994; and US$40 million in 1995. The average annual
unit cost of maintenance per kilometer of road are based on information provided by SECOPT on
recent contract prices. These unit costs are considered reasonable for budgeting purposes. Costs
range from US$2500-3000 for routine maintenance of paved roads including patching of road and
shoulders, clearing of right-of-way, cleaning and minor repairs of drainage, repair and maintenance of
traffic signs, etc. Equivalent cost for unpaved roads ranges from US$1500 to 2400. Periodic
maintenance uiit costs per km range from US$7000 for fog seal, US$15,000-20,000 for slurry seals
and single surface treatments and US$29,000-34,000 for double surface treatments. In the case of
unpaved roads, the cost of regravelling ranges from US$9000 to US$14,000. Depending on a
number of factors including traffic volume and climatic conditions, periodic maintenance operations
are carried out once every 4 to 7 years.
Project Priorities - Road Subsector
20.   The highest priority need in the road siubsector, as reflected in SECOPT's 1993-1996
Transport Sector Expenditure Program, is to continue with the on-going road network rehabilitation
efforts and to fully implement a comprehensive maintenance program which should reflect the policy
of the current administration of contracting with the private sector two thirds of the work. That
should be accompanied by an equally important effort of strengpthening the technical and institutional
capabilities of DGTT, DGC and DGCCA through technical assistance projects. The short term
priorities are (see also Table 5): (a) rehabilitation of the San Pedro Sula-Puerto Cortes (45 kIn) to

improve road access to the main port; (b) rehabilitation and widening of San Pedro Sula-EI Progreso
(25 km); (c) rehabilitation and widening of VUlanueva-Chamelecon (16 km) to increase the capacity
of this road which carries the heaviest traffic in the country particularly between San Pedro Sula and
the International Airport serving this city; (d) rehabilitation of the Pan American Highway (156 Km)
which is part of the Central America highway corridor. This road connects Honduras with the
neighboring countries of El Salvador and Nicaragua; (e) reconstruction of some sections of La
Entrada-Nueva Ocotopeque (165 tii:. This road forms part of an alternate Central America highway
corridor and provides access to Puerto Cortes for El Salvador and Guatemala; (f)
reconstruction/rehabilitation of Tegucigalpa-Catacamas (212 kn) to improve access to the eastern part
of the corridor; (g) reconstruction/rehabilitation of some sections of La Entrada-Copan Ruinas (62
kIn) to improve access to the tourist site at Copan; (h) paving of Siguatepeque-La Esperanza (66 km)

linking the Central American countries; 0) rehabilitation/periodic maintenance of Tela-La Ceiba (96

road and to seal the remaining of its length; (k) construction of Guasaule Bridge to provide connection
between Honduras and Nicaragua (a temporary 1-line bridge has been built in the interim); Q)
construction/rehabilitation of 350 km of feeder roads to improve access to markets for agricultural
products; and (m) road maintenance program.

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
THE BRIDGE DEPARTNET
EVALUATION OF INSTITUTIONAL AND TECICAL CAPABILiTIES
I. The  an ton of the Bridge Function in SECOPT
1.    The current organizaion structure of the "Direccion General De Caminos" fragments the
management of bridge design, maintnance and construction. The following discussed how the five
departments presendy control the operations related to the design, construction and maintenance of
structures on the road system:
(a) Tbe Stru     Dqpartmt
The organization structure of this department consists of a section Head, two engineers,
two technicians, one secretary and one driver. The department is understaffed and does
not have the proper expertise, support or power to carry its mandate. The department
occupying three rooms is poorly equipped and uses antiquated computing and filing
facig' ies. The staff has little exposure to modem methods of bridge design and most of
their experience is related to few types of standard concrete structures.
The main function of the Structures Department is to arrange for having typical bridge
designs available for the construction of new bridges. They have no direct control on
the mantenance and construction function of bridges, or on bridge related assignment
given by other departments to consultants.
(b) The Road Coutructlon Dqrtment
This department has no engineers with direct bridge design experience. Depending on
available funds and expertise, the Road Construction Department have the authority to
award to consultants or to the Structures Department, the design of new bridges. If a
design is awarded to a consulting firm, the firm will have the complete freedom to
establish its own design without the approval of the Structures Department. The
supervision of bridge construction is normally given to a consultant who also reports to
the Construction Department and prepares all contract documents (there is no Contract
Preparation Department for all SECOPT, each defrutment prepares its own contracts.)
(c) The Specbl Projects Dqermet (Modular Bridges)
This is a special unit for the fabr:ation of wood trss bridges used on rural roads.  mhe
unit consists of one section head, an engineer and 8 construction workers. The wood
engineer has his office within the Structures Department, however, ht -:ports to the hoad



- 82 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 12
of the Modular Bridges unit.
(d) The BID-BIRF Loan Department
This Department controis the disbursement of loan funds from the international lending
agencies. The unit is very powerfil and looks like an organization within the
organization. Although this Department has no bridge engineers, bridge works funded
by this unit are handed over to consultants without necessarily involving the Structures
Department. Contract documents and drawings may or may not be delivered to the
Structures Department after the bridges are built.
(e) The Maintenance Deparlment
This Department, supported by the maintenance staff in the districts, is responsible of the
maintenance of roads and bridges. However there is not a single person in this
department who is qualified to inspect, evaluate or rehabilitate a bridge.
There is no bridge inspection and rating system at SECOPT. It appears that br!dges get
inspected when the damage is very obvious or severe. This is largely due to the fact that
maintenance for bridges has been neglected and that the maintenance department is
ill-equipped to handle the task. There is evidence pointing to the fact that a great deal of
money could be saved if bridge maintenance was handled with professional attention.
I. BRIDGE INVENTORY
2.     Although there is no recording system for the inventory of structures on the 12,000 km of
road and highway system, a limited summary of the inventory of bridges compiled in 1986 by
SECOPT shows the number of bridges on the primary, secondary and rural highway system as being
338, 194 and 357 respectively. Recent unofficial estimates for the number of bridges on the primary
highway system was in the 600 range. About 238 structures have been identified in a 1992 report as
part of a bridge rehabilitation study. Another table of 65 structures was also supplied in a separate
document.
3.     Municipal bridges are not handled by SECOPT. The relation between the municipalities and
SECOPT is not formalized.
4.    It is also estimated that the number of pipe or box culverts per km of road varies between 1
(southern region) to 4 in the northern region. The great majority of these structures were built after
1970.



- 83 -
ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 12
m. TYPES OF HIGHWAY STRUCTURES
5.     Highway bridges in Honduras are not specifically designated to a certain type of traffic. It is
safe to assume that all bridges can be used for any type of traffic whether it is trucks, light vehicles,
school or transit buses, pedestrians, bicycles or animals. The majority of existing structures are made
of concrete, built over 25 years ago with less than three spans and an average span length less than
20.0 m.
6.     The majority of structures on the highway system are of the standard type set by the
Structural Department of SECOPT. Standard plans for typical bridge spans up to 30.0 m are used for
about 80% of the new structures. The remaining 20% of the structures are non-standard and are
primarily designed by consultants.
7.     The expensive cost of imported steel has almost eliminated the construction of new steel
bridges, However few older long span truss bridges were constructed around the 1940's. Most of
these bridges are showing signs of distress due to lack of maintenance.
Standard Stuctural Types
(a) Concrete Bridges. The majority of bridges are made of reinforced concrete slab on
reinforced concrete girders. Post-tensioned AASHTO type girders are used for spans
from 25.0 m to 30.0 m. All spans are simply supported.
Bridge abutments are normally constructed using a reinforced concrete L shaped beam
acting as a ballast wall as well as a beam cap supporting the superstructure. The beam is
either supported on a tapered stone bearing wall or on concrete piles. Neoprene bearing
pads, 1" thick, are used to support each girder. Intermediate supports are made of
reinforced concrete hammerhead type wall.
The bridges have inadequate detailing practice, particularly in the earth retaining
substructure, railing system, expansion joints, drainage system, approach slabs and slope
protection. The design of the deck slab does not take advantage of modem methods of
structural analysis to reduce tLe percentage of steel on the deck, while the design of the
girders does not take advantage of the continuity of the spans to reduce the material in
the structure.
Most of the current maintenance problems are due to the fact that many bridges were
built or rehabilitated using the oame inadequate detailing practice.
(b) Wood Bridges. Few truss wood bridges have been used by SECOPT in the last 9 years
fbr bridge spanning less than 24 m on the secondary road system. These bridges are
fabricated by the Puentes Modulares section of SECOPT. The current cost of a 15 m
bridge is 15% less than a comparable one build with reinforced concrete material.
Considering the superior performance and functicnRlity of concrete bridges, it is not
advisable to continue building these short span woud bridges until a proper condition
investigation is undertaken. The cost of the substructure component accounts for about



ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 12
50% of the total cost of a short span wood bridges; the other 50% is primarily governed
by the cost of few steel gusset plates. It appears advisable to use these bridges for the
longer spans (20 m - 25m) and to find a cheaper way to purchase steel sheets of smaller
sizes to fabricate gtuset plates.
(c) Culverts. Small culverts are made of concrete pipes ranging in diameter between 18" to
72". The majority of culverts spanning between 2.0 m to 3.0 m and having a clear
height between 2.0 m to 3.0 m, are made using a simple concrete slab supported on
stone bearing walls. Modern reinforced concrete single cell box are also used. Standard
culvert designs appear to have not been updated since 1976.
Few multiple box culverts have been built. Most, however, appear to have shear
deficiency due to an inadequate design of the haunch size at the connection between the
vertical walls and the top slab.
IV. DESIGN SPECIFICATIONS
8.    The current practice is to design bridges using AASHTO loading HS 20-44; however most of
the existing bridges were designed over 20 years ago using HS 15-44 loading. There is no record
showing which bridges were designed with what loading specifications. The axle load regulation
enacted in 1976 and enforced by some 14 control stations specifies for 15 truck configurations, the
axle weight and total permissible truck load. However, there was no study done to evaluate the
impact of load regulation on the safety of existing structures and on the adequacy of current bridge
design loading. Giving the age of the existing structures, it is likely that a large number of them are
substandard and need to be posted for lower loading.
V. BRIDGE REHABILITATION
9.     Bridge rehabilitation work is badly needed in Honduras. The extent of this work could only
be realized when all bridges have been surveyed, inspected and evaluated.
10.    The lack of funds, good design and rehabilitation standards as well as technical expertise to
perform prompt evaluation could be detrimental to the success of future rehabilitation projects.
VI. LOCAL EXPERTISE AND BRIIGE DESIGN PRACTICE
11.    The curet bridge design practice in Honduras is not taking advantage of the available
modern technology. This is no surprise since none of the bridge engineers at SECOPT has had
adequate exposure outside Honduras. The few engineers in Hondtras who had outside exposure are
working as private consultants to consulting firms and are not in a position to influence a change in
SECOPT. There is also concern about the level of instruction to future bridge designers in the local



- 85 -
MAN 4
Page S of 12
universities. Scores of bridges have failed to perform due to bad design practice an issue that will be
address under the project through the development of a Structural Standards Manual (para. 16).
12.    Until recently when several bridges have collapsed due to scour and sedimentation or
settlement activities at the bridge site, bridge hydrology and soil investigation studies have never been
a concern to bridge designers in Honduras. It is no surprise that the majority of required bridge
rehabilitation in Honduras, is due <x the practice of planning bridge sites and geometry without
studying the hydrology at the sitr,. It appears that existing expertise in bridge hydrology is limited
and the hydrological data is insufficient. To make things worst, the design of bridge substructure and
of slope protection is deficient.
VII. TECHNCAL ASSISTANCE FOR THIE IMPROVEMIENT OF THE BRIDGE FUNCTION
Objecdives
13.    Organkation Stuctue. Creation of a new Structural Department with strong mandates to
mane all functions related to bridges design, standards, construction, maintenance, management
system and archiving of bridge documents.
14.    The new department would have permanent competent technical staff to maintain the system
strong and efficient. It would use help from consultants as much as possible, maintain expertise by
doing at least 5% of complex work and shall always have the power and capability to manage the
bridge function. It would be supported by the districts staff to maintain its operation.
15.    The above recommendation would be difficult to achieve without changing the image of the
organization, equip the bridge function and create incentives to attract a competent bridge engineer to
lead the task.
16.    Bridge Standards. A new set of bridge standards produced by a committee of competent
bridge designers would be produced and published as a Structural Standards Manual. The manual
should be based on modern design practice with due consideration to local conditions. The manual
would address all procedures related to the preparation of drawings and contact documents of bridges
and would include drawings for the approved standard details of bridge components. Th. standards
would be used by aU bridge designers in Honduras, and would be maintained and updated by the
Structural Department.
17.    Bridge Mangement. A bridge management section within the Structures Department would
be created to take charge of the inentory records, inspection, maintenance, evaluation and
rehabilitation of the existing strucures. The new bridge management section would coordinate its
activities with the road management section and would be equipped to quickly respond to the needs
anywhere in Honduras. This new section would be responsible of setting its bridge management
standards, training personnel to inspect bridges and of supervising consultants, approving contracts
and evaluating priorities based on available funds.
18.   DocumenMent. A document management procedure would be established to
ensure the availability and secrit of design and drawing records at all times. A survoy of xciting



- 86 -
ANNEX 4
Page 6 of 12
records would be made and all effort would be made to collect missing records.
19.    Contract Managemet. To maintain consistency and quality assurance, the process of
preparing and awarding bridge contracts would be standardized, irrespective of the source of
funding.
20.    Education and Training Progam. A program would be set to improve the technical
capabilities of bridge engineers and technicians. This may involve exchanging information in
international conferences, training programs for engineers inside and outside Honduras, acquiring
modern computerized bridge programs and setting a good educational bridge program for engineering
students in universities.
Vm. TERM OF REFRENCE FOR THE DESIGN AND EIrMMATION OF BRIDGE
MANAGEMENT SYSIEM
21.    SECOPT intends to develop and implement a bridge management system (BMS) to assist in
handling the ever increasing number of functionally or structurally deficient structures which need
rehabilitation or replacement.
22.    An effective bridge management system will help SECOPT to organize the activities related
to the planning, designing, construction, maintenance, rehabilitation and replacement of bridges. Also
a BMS should a-sist management in defining the optimum cost effective solutions to achieve the
required level of services within the allocated fumds and to identify future funding requirements.
23.    The system will be managed by SECOPT with the Cooperation of consultants.
24.   The following describe the terms of reference for the technical assistance to develop and
implement the components of the bridge management system.
Define Organiion Chart for the Bridge Function
25.    The consultant shall in consultation with SECOPT management define, for each phase of the
implementation of the BMS, the functions to be carried out and, assign positions on the organization
chart and define the mandate and requirement for each position. The role of consultants, district staff
and Head office shall be identified. Based on this chart, SECOPT shall assign a task force which will
assist the consultant in developing and implementing the components of the bridge management
system.
The following processes most be addressed by the new organization:
(a) Structural Standards and Procedures.
(b) Design aids and computational support
(c) Records storage.
(d) Structural planning process.

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
The Ports Subsector
I. INTRODUCTION
1.   The Government of Honduras has undertaken the implementation of an important program of
economic reforms, with emphasis on export-led growth, the liberalization of foreign trade and the
encouragement of investment by the private sector. These' measures represent important challenges
for the port sector, which has to improve efficiency and expand capacity in order to meet increased
traffic and, on the institutional side, to adopt those changes that will encourage increased participation
by the private sector and thus faster reaction to users needs.
2.   The port sector, especially Puetro Cortes, has also the opportunity to generate additional
revenues for the country, through the creation of the right conditions of efficiency and price that will
encourage the development of the port as a regional distribution center for transshipment traffics, thus
serving as a regional distribution center. The port sector has also an important role to play in the
encouragement of the cruise ship segment of the tourist industry.
3.   In order to achieve these objectives, the port industry and port institutions, both public and
private, have to define and to adopt reforms and improvements, especially in the areas of
administration, operation, tariffs and investment.
H. PRESENT POSITION
Ports
4.   Honduras has six commercial ports. Five are on the Atlantic coas;. ?uerto Cortes, Tela, La
Ceiba and Puerto Castilla/Trujillo, with Roatan serving the Bay islands. The port complex of San
Lorenzo/Amapala is on the Pacific coast. The ENP is owner also of a number of other assets,
principally land at Tela, La Ceiba and Isla del Tigre. No precise figures were available on the area
of these lots and it is understood that in some cases, there is doubt concerning title.
Port Administaion
5.   The ports are administered by the Empresa Nacional Portuaria (ENP). This is an autonomous
public entity, established by Law No. 40 (16 Oct 1965), whose objective is "national economic
development, by means of the provision of adequate and efficient services and installations in the



- 94 -
Page 2 of 9
ports". The ENP received the assets and liabilities of Puerto Cortes in 1966, followed by those of
Tela. La Ceiba, Trujillo, Roatan and Amapala in 1974. San Lorenzo and Puerto Castilla were
constructed by the ENP during the period 1973 - 1921, to form the port complexes of San
Lorenzo/Amapala and Puerto Castilla/Trujillo respectively.
6.    The Board of Directors (Consejo Directorio) of the ENP includes the Ministers of the Economy
(who acts as President of the Board), of Transport, of Natural Resources and of the Secretariat of
Planning, Coordination and Budget, together with representatives of the Navy, the Chambers of
Commerce and Industry, organized labor and the national shipowners and agents. The General
Manager of the ENP is also a (non-voting) member of the Board. Among the responsibilities of the
ENP is to advise the 'Jovernment on port policy and there is no other office within Government
specifically concerned with port policy. Within the ports, control of ship entry and exit and the
control of pollution is the responsibility of the Port Captains (who report to the Direccion General de
la Marina Mercante, which in turn is part of the Navy). Other official bodies (Customs, Health and
Quarantine, Immigration, etc.) also intervene in the ports with their customary functions.
Ship Cal
7.    Ship calls in 1991 totalled 1,538, of which 1,445 were at Atlantic coast ports and 93 at San
Lorenzo on the Pacific coast. Container (Li/Lo) and Roll on/Roll off (Ro/Ro) vessels predominated,
with totals of 523 and 322, (34 % of all calls Li/Lo and 21 % Ro/Ro) respectively. A small
proportion of vessels have both Li/Lo and Ro/Rol' capabiiity and are classified as container vessels.
Puerto Cortes was the busiest port with 1195 calls, followed by Puerto Castilla with 187 calls. Tabie
1 gives details for 1990 and 1991.
Port Traffic
8.    Port traffic has increased from 2.32 million tons in 1975 to 3.59 million tons in 1991, which
represents an annual average growth of 2.8 %. Imports have increased from 1089 thousand tons to
1793 thousand tons over the same period, with an average annual growth rate of 3.2 % and exports
have grown from 1233 thoulsand tons to 1680 thousand tons, with an average annual growth of 1.9
%. The imports figure for 1991 includes an item "carga en transito" of 238 thousand tons. The
above figures also include a relatively small tonnage of coastal traffic, principally outward from
Puerto Cortes/La Ceiba to the Bay islands and to the province of Mosquitia, amounting to about 50
thousand tons per year.
9.    Traffic classified according to its form of presentation is shown in Table 2 for the period 1980
through 1991. Oil imports represent the single largest item of import cargo, followed by general
cargo. For exports, "specialized cargo" (pfedominately bananas) is the largest single category,
followed by general cargo. Dry bulks together make up about 20% of all traffic, excluding liquids.
[/    Li/Lo and Ro/Ro refer to the method of cargo handling usd by the ship. LiLo or 'Lif on /lift off' means that the containes
are lifted on and off the ship by ceane (which may be ether on the ship or on the quay), whilst Ro/Ro or 'Roil on Roll off' means
that the cargo eteo and leaves the ship on tralers or some other wheeled equipment.

truck, for example from/to Nicaragua, presents further ontions, as does the movement of goods to
and from Free Zones within Honduras. The 1991 statistics for Puerto Cortes record a total of 260
thousand tons as "carga en transito", of which 68 thousand tons are recorded as "transbordo", i.e.
with destinations outside Ionduras. The remainder, 192 thousand tons, is classified as "redestino"; a
substantial proportion of this, probably 60 or 70% is Free Zone traffic.
15. Port statistics record three types of containerized and Ro/Ro traffic: "'ontainerized", handled as
lift on /lift off cargo, "trailers", which are integral box units with their own wheels and "chassis",
which are platforms or skeletal trailers, also with their own wheels21. Trailers and chassis are both
handled by Roll on/Roll off methods. The relative proportions of each type are shown in Table 4, for
units with cargo, empties and totals, for the years 1990 and 1991. ENP ports handled an estimated
106 thousand units in 1991, of which 84 thousand were containers and 22 thousand were trailers or
chassis. This is equivalent to approximately 123,000 TEU3' and 1.1 million tons of cargo.
Shipping Services
16. The principal international shipping services calling at Honduran ports are listed in Table 5 .
With the exception of Puerto Cortes and the specialized services for bananas, the average cargo
handled per ship call is relatively small and this implies that, especially on the Pacific coast, the
importance of the call for the shipowner is similarly modest. This factor, coupled with the
competition from nearby ports, indicates the necessity to take all possible measures to increase the
commitment of the shipowner to his Honduran port call, by either the potential of capturing additional
freight or by contracual obligations to local interests.
Port Imallalions and Equipment
17.  Port installations at the principal ENP ports are listed in Table 6. The investment program for
1992-1993 includes the reconstruction of berth IA (bulk liquids) in Puerto Cortes, the rehabilitation
of the jetty at Tela and relatively minor repairs to the berth and to the ex-timber shed at Puerto
Castdlla.
18. Ihe principal items of fixed and mobile cargo-handling and of floating equipment are shown in
Table 7. Ihe main items of fixed equipment are the gantry crane for container handling and the
banana loading elevators, both at Puerto Cortes. The gantry crane was out of action for much of
1991, following a ship impact but is now back in service. The trailing cable has been slightly
shortened, so dtht the crane is restricted to about 147ft less than its full run. There is also at Puerto
Cortes an unfinished cement installation consisting of five silos and an incomplete elevated conveyor
between silos and quay. Ownership of this installation has, it is believed, passed to the government
and a technical inspection should be made, to identify options and costs for its future use. Other than
the cement conveyor, there is no specialized equipment for the handling of dry bulks.

19.  The mobile cargo-handling equipmfAt consists of that rquired primarily for container handling
(straddle carriers, a top-lifter and tractors/chassis), for heavy lifts (truck mounted cranes) and also
which is needed for general cargo (forldift trucks, tractors and trailers). The heavy lift and gmneral
cargo equipment has an average age of 8-10 years and is available in sufficient quantitios to make up
for a down-time of about 35%, which is the average for the smaller equipment and somewhat less for
the larger machines. Most problems have been e,countered with the container handling equipment.
The straddle carriers include two Rubery Owen machines, about 24 years old and of which normally
only one is working; and two Nelcons, about 7 years old, which have suf,ared an engine
modification, and again normally widi one available. There is also a 45 ton top-lifter, whose
operation is obviously difficult to combine efficiently with the straddle carriers. Problems have also
been encountered with the comaim  tractors and 8 new items are currently being commissioned. The
floating equipment includes tugs, pilot launches and a high capacity cutter suction dredger, which is
much underutilized.
20. There is one workshop for civil engineering maintenance and four workshops for mechanical
maintenance: container handling equipment, general equipment, routine maintenance and a machine
shop. The usual problems are encountered with the shortage of spare parts and this has impeded the
implementation of preventative and planned maintenance schemes, so that the current practice is to do
first line routine maintnance, with breakdown and accident repair when the occasion arises. Record
keeping is on a job by job basis, with little synthesis of the data from individual machines in order to
provide management statistics. Work has been done entirely in the ENP workshops. Recently, as a
new policy, a contract has been given for the general overhaul of 6 tractors to a private firm. Cost
estimate for the first two items have been recently received, substantially higher than expected and are
under analysis. The division of maintenance in this way between ENP and outside workshops for the
same items of equipment tends to confuse responsibility for keeping the machines in good working
order. There is a proposal for the unification of the mechanical workshops in a new Central
Workshop to be constructed, although this does not appear to be appropriate for current policy
regarding the future role of the ENP in cargo handling, nor with the future shape of port operations,
as discussed below.
Organizaion of operations: marine operaions
21.  Vessels are cleared inwards by the Official VisitW, normally between 6am and 6pm, with
additional charges outside these hours. The use of ENP pilot and tug (or tugs) is compulsory for
vessels avor 300grt. Their use is not charged for separately and is included in the overall charge for
the use of the port. A launch service is provided privately. Clearance outwards is authorized by
Customs and the Port Captain, with an additional charge when this is done outside ncrmal hours.

Organization of operations: cargo handlUg['
22.  Ship work (stevedoring) is done by the private sector. This includes both work on the ship and
associated work on shore such as the (onsolidation of LCLCI containers, the making up of pallets for
export cargo and the operation of specialized equipment for banana loading. Labor is hired by the
ships agents whose practice is to have permanent supervisory staff and to make up the ga igs from
casual labor. We understand that no formal system exists for the organization of this labor.
23.  Work on shore (porterage, or the movement of cargo so and from the ship's side, shed work
and the reception an.  delivery of cargo to iand transport) is done by ENP employees or by casual
labor hired by the ENP. Normal working hours for ENP labor are 7am to 4.30pm with a lunch
break of 30 minutes. These hours include two hours of compulsory overtime. Additional charges arc.
made for continuous or overtime work (Saturday afternoon, Sunday and night work). Relatively high
charges for dockage encourage the use of overtime, despite its approximately double cost.
Productivity figures fo; different categories of vessel are given in Table 8. The figures for dry bulks
are low, due to the lack of specialized equipment; also for timber, which is handled from rail wagon
to ship. Other figures, whilst not high, are in line with regional averages for the better ports. For
the purpose of this report a sample of ships analyzed for the container and Ro/Ro berth, giving
productivities as follows:
Ro/Ro: 16-18 moves per hour
Li/Lo using port crane: 12-17 moves per hour
Li/Lo using ship gear: 16-31 moves per hour
These figures, though naturally depending on the type of ship and on the total number of moves,
show room for improvement. The container terminal, apart from the equipment shortcomings referred
to above, also suffers from an outdated and inadequate manual control system, which will require
replacing by a modern electronic one.
24.  Tallying (checking) of cargo is done (for imports) at shipside then, on completion of the ship,
in the transit shed and finally on delivery to land transport. Tally is done by ship agent's personnel,
Customs and the ENP.
25.  Similar arrangements for cargo handling operations apply at the other ports with variations
whicL reflect their special conditions. At Tela, the shore work is done by a cooperative formed from
previous employees of the Tela Company, so that the role of the ENP is that of supervision and the
collection of charges. At Puerto Castilla, extensive use is made of hired equipment and drivers for
tht movement of containers to and from shipside. At San Lorenzo there is a substantial ENP
operational work force. At present low levels of traffic, demand for labor is necessarily irregular;
this, coupled with the collective agreement, may result in the labor force being without work all day;
then, if a ship docks at 5pm, it will have to pay overtime rates if it is to start work that night. Such
practices are not appropriate for a small port attempting to attract cargo.
1   'he desiption is of practices at Puerto Corts; wher practices at tba other ports difer, reference is nede below.
gi   LCL: Lmg than Contin eLoad: wher tcA container contains various consgnntns, with different ownes.
