Through transport
26. Only one shipping line maintains an inland terminal at Tegucigalpa, so that containers can be
taken from the port for deconsolidation at a terminal nearer the destination of the goods. The
probtem here appears to be one of road transport costs rather than any impediment from Customs for
inland clearance, so tLat in most cases, consignees prefer to send their own transport to the port to
collect their goods, instead of collecting them from an inland terminal (and paying the cost of the
inland movement of the container).
Role of the private and public sectors in Puerto Cortes
27.  In Puerto Cortes, the role of the private sector in the provision of services within the port is
limited to the provision of stevedoring services, plus the provision of certain specialized services,
such as the launches. There are in addition a number of off-dock private container terminals within
the town. The private sector's participation in port investment or in the" provision of port equipment
is at present minimal. Equipment, shorehandling and most marine services are provided by the ENP,
as is practically all investment.
ENPs Financial Position
28.   Although ENP as a whole has constantly made operational surpluses (see ratios below), the
large investments in infrastructure made from 1966 onwards, has created a heavy debt burden in
recent years. Nevertheless, by keeping some port charges high, ENP has managed to continue
servicing its debts as well as supporting the weaker ports and the Free Zones. It also contributes 4%
of its revenues to the port cities for their development budgets. As may be seen from the figures, the
trend from 1989 onwards however, indicates a worsening of the financial situation. An important
factor in the growing working costs has been the relatively large proportion of administrative costs
which have continued to grow much faster than direct operational costs (see diagram below). Tables
9 and 10 present ENP's most recent revenue account and balance sheet.
29. ENP's Working and Operating Ratios for the last years were as follows:
1987      1988       1989       1990        1991
Working Ratio  (%)         54        53         55         60          64
|Operating Ratio (%j        71        70          70         73         78 1
Note: Working Ratio = Total operating expenses less depreciation/Operating Revenues
Operating Ratio = Total operating expenses (including depreciation)/Operating
Revenues



- 100-
Page 8 of 9
These operating ratios need to be viewed with serious reservation, as depreciation (which accounts for
the difference between the working and operpting ratios) has not been adjusted to reflect the
considerable recent devaluation of the Lempira. As depreciation does not therefore represent the up-
dated values of the use of the installations and equipment, it means that profits are considerably
overstated. In the balance sheet, because the equity value is understated, the apparent leverage (debt
of equity ratio) is also overstated.
30.   Even if one allows for the understated capital value, the ENPs balance sheet shows a fairly
precarious financial position. The enterprise has heavy capital obligations, with short term interest
payments accounting for about a third of the total annual operating revenue. Nor are the current ratios
d-sirable ones. This situation stems pardy from the considerable investments made by ENP in a
relatively short time-span to construct the country's port and Free Zone infrastructure.
Tariffs and Costs
31.   There is little relation between specific port costs for different operations and the charges for
such operations, as cargo-handling charges and wharfage have not been changed since 1982, whereas
costs have risen in the last ten years. Cbarges to ships however, became "dollarised" (and actually
collected in dollars at a rate of L 2.0 to the dollar) since November 1990. ENP has obtained special
dispensation to use its dollar reve_ es for its foreign exchange transactions e.g. the purc. ase of
equipment and spare parts and repayment of foreign loans. The remaining balance after such
transactions is sold to the Central Bank at the current exchange rate (L.5.40 to the dollar). The
Revenue Statements (which are in Lempiras) therefore show a very large income from the exchange
transaction, which ought really to be classified as revenue from ship services. Although this is not
immediately apparent from the financial statements, ship operators maintain that vessel charges in
Honduras are the highest in the Latin American region - which is confirmed by a comparison of
vessel disbursement accounts. (See example in Table 11).
32.  As ENP has an absolute monopoly, it can maintain its charges not only to reflect its total
operating costs, but also to cover any other obligations the government has thrust on it, like the 4%
of revenues (gross) for the municipalities and transfers to the government as requested and budgeted
from time to time e.g. a transfer of L. 10 million (about $ US 2 million) in 1991. The extent to
which excessive charges are effectively a barrier to trade has not been calculated, but there seems no
doubt that when the option of cheaper neighboring ports is realized (Barrios and Santo Tomas on the
Atlantic in Guatemala and Corinto or Acajutla 5 tLie Pacific in Nicaragua and El Salvador
respectively), traffic at the margin will be diverted to these ports.
Futue inancing
33.  The interest shown by the private sector in greater participation in ENPs activities could help
mobilize the capital required to help service the debt burden and improve the financial status of the
ports while at the same time rendering them more efficient, through a better use of existing assets -
which are currendy underutlized. By broadening its equity base through the participation of private
capital in some of ENP operations, which would require the setting up of subsidiary companies of



- 101 
mlweo public and private caphl, firther Indebtedness would be avoided and greater commitment to
the economic uilization of the easses would be created.
Free Zones
34.  'Me ENP has been responsible for the very successif developmet of thr Free Zone at Puerto
Cortes, which probably rprosn the scond largest private secter source of crployment in the
country, after the ban    Industry. After a some-lhat slow start, this zone ls now beginning to sihow
an appropriate return on the investments made. 'rne ENP has also been responsihle for the
development of similar though much smaller zones at Tela and La Ceiba. In add; >n it i. responsible
for the administration of similar zones at Choloma and Omoa. Tbe zcnes employ approximately
13,000 people and genert an es_atd  US$80 millions per year in sales. There is a measure under
active consideration to remove the zones from the ENP and to call for bids for them from the private
sector.
35.  It is important that a clear distnction be made between those free zones that are adjacent or in
dlose proximity to deep water, a is the case for the free Zone in Puerto Cortes, and those that are at
inland locations. Deep water sites are intrinsically scarce and should in principle be reserved for
those industries that hae need for them. Inland sites are not limited and a different and more passive
policy may be adopted to industries wishing to locate in them. In any caso, land adjacent to daep
water should not, aeept in specal c3rcumstances, be sold for private development but rather leased,
on terms which assure the optidmal use of such areas.

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Reforms of the Port Sector - lrst Phase
Terms of Reference
I.   Objectives
The consultant will work under the guidance of the IC, to which he will report administratively
and technically. Within the agreed preliminary program for the restructuring of the port
sector, he/she will work with the Empresa Nacional Portuaria (ENP) in the evaluation of the
program.
H.   Q calo,
The consultant should bave a wide experience of different port organizations and have played a
significant role in the definition and implementation of change in port organization and
especially in the sector of cargo handling operations.
M. Duralion
Approximately nine weeks.
IV.  Tasks to be Undertaken
He/she will evaluate the relative merits of the different schemes such as leasing, concessioning
or other forms of participation by the private sector in port areas and/or activities.
He/she will assess the financial impact of the proposed privatization schemes on ENP, the
relevant municipalities and on the Government's budget.
He/she will evaluate the economic costs and benefits for feasible privatization alternatives.
He/she will initiate the preparation, in discussion with the ENP, of a revised organizational
chart for that organization, in line with its proposed role.
He/she will prepare recommendations for the steps necessary to reach that new organizational
structure, including such training and retraining as may be necessary and identifying the
principal changes required in the regulatory framework.



- 119-
ANNEX S
Attahment
Page 2 of 8
He/she will identify the work to be carried out in the implementation phase by the consultants
that would work in the areas of concessions and leases, cargo handling equipment and
workshops, port structures and manpower planning.



- 120-
ANNEX S
Attaehment
Pasge 3 of 8
HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Reforms of the Port Sector
Terms of Reference - Finandal An*s
Objectives
The consultant will work as a member of the technical assistance team. He/she will work on a
day-to-day basis with the Empresa Nacional Portuaria (ENP) in the analysis of the implications
of the Action Plan agreed during the first phase of the technical assistance for the income,
expenditure and profits of the ENP. Based on such analysis, he/she will prepare an outline
Fin&ncial Plan. This will define the impact on the ENP of the ENP of the policy of
concessions, coupled with the proposed reorganization and consequential reduction in ENP
staffing needs. The outline Financial Plan will be expressed in mdel form, so that the effect
of changes in levels of revenue from the concessions, in tariffs and in manpower policy, can be
assessed.
H1. QualUications

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Reforms of the Port Sector - Implementation Phase
Terms of Reference - Specialist in Concessions and Leases
I.   Objectives
The consultant will work as a member of the technical assistance team, reporting
administratively to the team coordinator. He/she will work in close coordination with the
Empresa Nacional Portuaria (ENP) on those aspects of the Action Plan concerned with the
transfer of port operations, equipment and services to the private sector by means of
concessions and licenses. Towards this objective he/she will prepare recommendations, on the
basis of his work, on the best definition, content and bidding arrangement for such concessions
and licenses, within an overall strategy that recognizes the need for the encouragement of a
competitive environment by means of a transparent bidding process. The recommendations
will be discussed prior to their finalization, with the team coordinator and with the IC.
Qualifications
The consultant should have extensive experience in the formulation and negotiation of
commercially based concessions and services in the port sector. He/she should have recent
high level managerial experience in a major port. He/she should preferably have language
capability in English and Spanish.
m.  Duration
The consultant will work for approximately 9 weeks, including three visits to Honduras a-ad
two weeks for the preparation and revision of documents at his/her home office.
IV.  Tasks to be undertaken
The consultant will travel to Honduras for an initial period of approximately three weeks
during which he/she will make a physical examination of the activities of the ports, in order to
prepare recommendations for the best classification of concessions and licenses into a small
number of groups. The general idea is to allocate the major berths/areas on the basis of
concessions through competitive bidding, leaving certain berths as public berths, with licensed
stevedores. The precise strategy will be defined during the mission.



- 123 -
ANNEX S
Attachweit
lWe 6of 8
For each group he/she wIll then prepare bidding documents suitable for public competitive
bidding based on the prequalification of suitable firms, with an objective evaluation process,
which is to be the subject of recommendation by the consultant. The documents will be
prepared in English.
He/she will travel to Honduras for a second visit in order to review with IC and ENP, the final
form of the draft documents, prior to their publication and to discuss and agree the procedures
to be employed for the prequalification of suitable bidders.
On his/her third visit, he will review with IC and ENP, the offers that have been received for
the initial bidding process, assisting in the process of evaluation, in accordance with the
procedures already agreed, incorporating any revision that are deemed necessary in the light of
experience.
His/her main task is the preparation of the draft bidding documents and the procedures to be
followed in the prequalification of the bidders and in the evaluation of the bids received.
He/she will also prepare a brief report after each visit, that will summarize his work, problems
encountered and solutions to those problems.

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABELITATION PROJECT
Techdical Assistance to the Aviation Subsector
Objectives.
1.        The technical assistance to the aviation subsector would be carried out in close
collaboration with the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO). The main objective of this
technical assistance is to support the airports' organizational restructuring in order to increase their
efficiency and modernize their management. In particular, this technical assistance would be geared
towards the creation of autonomous airports in charge of services to air traffic and related
infrastructure and other airport services, that would earn and dispose according to their own budget
the revenues from aeronautical and non-aeronautical fees.
Definition of Responsibilities.
(a)   ICAO, in the context of the UNDP-financed project for the development of civil
aviation in Honduras, would be responsible, inter alia, for the provision of technical
assistance for (i) the definition of the legal, regulatory and institutional framework for
the subsector; (ii) the economic and financial reorganization of civil aviation and
airports; (iii) airport privatization; (iv) the improvement of management information
systems; and (v) the revision of the regulations related to air safety.
(b)    The IDA-financed technical assistance under the proposed project would finance
experts to co-manage the Tegucigalpa and San Pedro Sula Airports during a two-year
period, which is expected to be the amount of time necessary to complete the
transition towards the new organizational system. In addition to familiarize the
technical staff with modern management practices and operational procedures to be
developed by ICAO, this technical assistance will help to make more transparent the
costs of the different airport services and, thus, permit a better ass3ssment of the
value of future concessions to the private sector.
ICAO's Technical Assistance for the Reorganization of the Civil Aviation Subsector.
Terns of Reference
In agreement with the Government, the consultants will:
(a)    define a new scheme for the organization of civil aviation in Honduras, including the
relationship of the airports with the Central government and the Directorate General
of Civil Aviation (DGCA), separating the normative, regulatory and monitoring
functions from those that are eminently operational in executing air traffic services
and airport infrastructure operation and maintenance;



- 127-
ANNEX6
Page 2 of 9
(1)    define the organizational structure for the airports of Toncontin (Tegucigalpa), La
Mesa (San Pedro Sula), Goloson (La Ceiba) and Roatan (Roatan). Determine for
each airport the services, installations, equipment and material under their
responsibility;
(c)   incorporate into the airport responsibilities those functions now under SECOPT's
Directorate General of Road and Airport Maintenance (DGCCA) and the Directorate
General of Civil Works and Urbanism;
(d)    determine the staffing and training needs for each airport according to the assigned
functions, including international training;
(e)   recommend the most appropriate legal arrangements for the airportL and the
mechanisms for their implementation;
(f)   in light of the organizational and financial structure agreed with the Government, draft
a proposed revision to the Civil Aviation Law in ord-r to provide the adequate legal
framework taking into account the following requirements: (i) the airports to earn
revenues from services, air tickets, and other sources; (ii) the airports to administer
their own budget; and (iii) organizations having installations and/or providing services
in the airports would be subordinated to the airport authority;
(g)    carry out an evaluation of the expected income and expenditures of the airports of
Toncontin, La Mesa, Goloson and Roatan in order to assess f,uture privatization
arrangements;
(h)    develop the regulations for charging airports fees and earning through concessions and
establish the operational procedures to apply those regulations;
(i)    design an airport and "in-route" cost-accounting methodology in order to determine
the cost of each service;
(j)    design a methodology to monitor demand for each type of service in order to define
unit-costs and consequently, airport and "in-route" tariffs;
(k)    propose a billing and collection system for airport services;
(1)    evaluate the Government contributions, if any, that will be required by the airports in
the next five years; and
(m)   assess the convenience of transferring to COCESNA the equipment for navigational
aid, in exchange for a fee, or alternatively, to establish additional charges for the use
of air space below 20000 feet.

Tecnical Asitance for the Improvement of Airport Administraffon
2.       Tbis technical assistance has as a main objective to provide support to airport managers,
on a co-managerial basis, in the operational and financial aspects of airport activities. The team of
experts will consist of a Team Leader, and two Operational Advisors. The Team Leader, in addition
to acting as the main advisor to the Directors of the airports of Tegucigalpa and San Pedro Sula, will
act as the financial advisor, with the responsibility of implementing ICAO systems for cost
accounting, billing, contracting, concessioning, tariffs, etc. The Operational Advisors, in close
cooperation with the Airports Operational Managers, would be responsible for the day-to-day
operation of the airports. The following paragraphs summarize the responsibilities of the consulting
team.
The Team Leader will serve as the principal advisor for the director of the Airports of
Tegucigalapa and San Pedro Sula. In this capacity he/she, in consultation with the Airport Director,
will be responsible for:
(a)  the overall operation of the airports and providing overall direction in accomplishment of the
mission of the airports;
(b)  seeing that all activity at the airports, whether by airlines, private sector, the public, or
personnel of the airports, is conducted in a safe, efficient and business-like manner;
(c) both airports' budgets, fiscal, personnel, data processing and management activities;
manag,ement of revenue producing contractual arrangements; acquisition of supplies and
materials; development of business and operational data and information;
(d) providing for the orderly amortization of the Government's investment, coupled with a
reasonable return on the capital investment in airport facilities; and
(e)  directly supervising his/her administrative staff and the Operations Managers of both airports.
PRINCIPAL DUTIES AND RESPONSIBILITIES
(a)  Directs all operations of the airports in a sound business manner. This involves:
(i)    participation in the establishment of overall business management policies and
philosophy of the airports;
(ii)   integration and coordination of business policies of the airports with operating
programs; approving basic programs and functions of various airport offices; and
providing program emphasis; and

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILTATION PROJECr
THE RAILWAY SUBSECTOR
PRESENT CONDMON OF THE HONDURAS NATIONAL RAILWAY
Component of the Railway Network
1.     The extension of the railway network in Honduras is 886 km. Out of it, 204 kn are currently
operated by Ferrocarriles Nacionales de Honduras (FNH) in the Valle de Sula region. Another portion
of the network, extending over 336 km in the same region, has been given in concession to a fruit
company (Tela Railroad Co., subsidiary of The United Fruit Co.). A l10 km railroad links the city of
Tela with Puerto La Ceiba. This section is also under FNH administration, but is currently out of
service. All these track segments have 42" gauge.
2.     The remaining 236 km extends from La Ceiba to the Rio Aguan valley, and are part of the
FNH's system. It is a 36" gauge railroad, and was formerly operated by the Standard Fruit Co. At
present this network is out of service due to a severe deterioration of its infrastructure and the lack of
railway transport demand. The following table summarizes the network sections, their gauges, the
operator and whether they are in service.
l            ~ Area  Legth    Gauge | Operator      Service
Valle de Sula     204 km      42"      FNH          Yes
Valle de Sula     336 km      42"  | TRR Co.        Yes
Tela - LaCeiba    110 km      42"      FNH          No
La Ceiba          236 km      36"      FNH          No
FNH:       Ferrocarril Nacional de Hondumn
mTR Co: Tela Railroad Company
Ferrocarril Nacional de Honduras Activities
3.     The FNH transports freight and passengers, and also performs some additional activities,
particularly yard movements for the TRR Co. wagons in Puerto Cortes.



-136-
ANNEX 7
Page 2 o 6
4.     Freight. In the Valle de Sula section (the only one now in operation), bananas are the primary
commodity. Wood, wheat and empty containers are the additional important products. In the 1986/90 five
years period, the average volume transported was the following:
Product      Volume (000 tn)       %
Bananas                   150.1         45.0
Wood                       50.3         15.1 
Wheat                      40.5         12.2
Others                     92.3         27.2
Total          1          333.2        100.0
5.     The average disne is 63 km; 77.3 km for bananas and 51.9 km for the other products. Out
of a total of 21.1 million of tons-km for the 1986/90 five years period, bananas represent 11.6 millions
tn-km (55%) and the other products 9.5 (45%). Table 1 shows the evolution of the freight activity in the
last 10 years. La Ceiba section was operating under concession up to 1984, but it has not had traffic since
mid 1989. Table 1 clearly depicts the declining freight trends.
6.     Bananas are generally loaded in Higuerito and Guanchias, 15 km south of San Pedro Sula, and
unloaded at Puerto Cortes. Wheat is loaded in Puerto Cortds and transported to several mills in the San
Pedro Sula area. Wood is loaded in the San Pedro Sula area and unloaded in Puerto Cortes.
7.     Passegers. FNH is transporting 165.000 passengers yearly (average 1989/90). The mean
distance is 25 kn, which amounts to 4.1 millions pas-km a year. The passenger service consist of one
train from San Pedro Sula to Puerto Cortds every morning, returning in the afternoon. Passengers use
intermediate stations intensively. The railroad is very close to the paved highway connecting Puerto
Cortds with San Pedro Sula Cm which a good bus service exists) for most of its run. It is the lowest
income people who use the railway, becaue its fare is about half the fare of the bus.
8.     There are two ypes of passenger cars. The lowest quality service consist in box cars adapted,
with some wooden benches and without windows. The first class cars have windows and seats. There are
some rail cars for special services, transporting tourist groups from San Pedro Sula to Puerto Cort6s.
9.     A summary of the FNH total annual activity in 1989/90, measured in thousand trains-km, shows
the following:

TOTAL TRAINS-KM                  343.8
Operational Organization
10.    Banana movements are the most important activity, and strongly influence the operational
organization. T1be fruit exporters, who are the main users of FNH, need to load the wagons in the
production area.and rapidly transport them to the port in order to coordinate the train and the ships
arrivals. 40' refrigerated containers on flat cars are widely used, as well as some box cars. The loaded
wagons are concentrated in the San Pedro Sula area, and the trains are dispatched to Puerto Cortes.
11.    Railway wagons should not stay in the port terminal more than 24 hours (in no case more than
48 hours) to avoid fruit deterioration. At port the containers are connected to the power system of the
terminal constructed by TRR Co. The main rail return freight is the transport of empty containers.
Plant Characteristfcs
12.    Power engines. In the Valle de Sula area FNH has 10 locomotives, 8 of which have 1050 HP,
one has 850 HP and one 350 HP. The locomotives have an average age of 27.5 years. Only six of them
are currently in use, three are used for freight trains, one for passenger trains, and two for yard
operations and are held as spare. The average utilization of the locomotives is 58.000 km/year, relatively
intensive for a short system. Maintenance works are carried on in the San Pedro Sula shop. It has very
modest facilities, and does not provided any programmed maintenance. There are no reliable records on
each locomotive nor available spare parts for routine maintenance.
13.    In La Ceiba sector there are 17 (36" gauge) locomotives, within the range of the 450 to 650 HP.
All of them are now out of service.
14.    Wagons and cars. At present there are 404 wagons in the Valle de Sula sector, with a capacity
close to 9500 tn. Out of them 172 are flat cars (for containers and wood), 133 are "rejillas" and 99 are
box cars. For passenger transportation, FNH has 7 cars and 3 motor cars. The average age of the fleet
(as it was in 1987) is presented in Table 2. The wagons and cars are maintained and eventally rebuilt
in La Laguna shop, close to Puerto Cortes, which is in an extremely bad condition. The frequent
derailments are because 40' containers, when fully loaded, are very heavy for the wagons, which cannot
turn correctly in the track curves.



- 138-
ANNEX 7
Page 4 of 6
15.    In La Ceiba sector there are 90 flat cars, 265 "rejillas",1 20 tank wagons and 20 box cars, as
well as 12 passenger cars (all 36" gauge).
16.    Infrastructure. The track shows some very deteriorated segments in the Valle de Sula sector.
Maintenance is made mostly by hand, and is insufficient. In the La Ceiba sector the tracks are out of
service because of the damage produced by heavy rainfalls. In the Tela-La Ceiba corridor the track is
also out of service because of natural hazards, and it is very difficult to turn back to operations because
a lot of material has been plundered.
17.    Other systems. Although a systematic analysis could not be carried out, the other supporting
systems (signaling, comrmunications) show the same characteristics as the other plan components: very
old material, with a very weak maintenance.
Revenues and Expenditures
18.    Table 2 shows the evolution of revenues and expenditures in the 1986/1990 period. Investment
expenditures have been almost negligible in the period. In the Valle de Sula sector the operational deficit
depicts an increasing trend, mainly due to a continuous deterioration of the railway revenues. This is the
result of the reduction in the tons transported and the erosion of the freight rates. In La Ceiba sector the
trend is similar up to 1989, when operations stopped. In 1990 there were almost no operational revenue.
19.    'Other revenues" includes the compensations paid by the fruit companies to FNH for concession
agreements or refunds for track repairs. In La Ceiba sector, the agreement was such that the transfer
became null when operations stop.
20.    As a result of all these factors, the total deficit of FHN has been increasing at a growing rate.
In 1990 this deficit amounted Lps. 7.2 million (US$ M 1.35), which was financed through governmental
transfers and real estate sales.
21.    Table 3 shows FNH revenues by source in 1990, as well as the expenditures classified according
to the most important items. Table 4 has a more detailed analysis of the expenditures, according to the
railway functions. Personnel represents 54% of the total expenditures (which reflects some overstaffing),
and parts and repairs 15 % (showing the weak maintenance). General administration costs are considerably
high by any standards.
Legal Frame and Internal Organization
22.    Ferrocarriles Nacionales de Honduras was created by Decreto Ndmero 48, in 1958, as a para-
statal organization. Although it is an independent entity, FNH reports to SECOPT. The decree
emphasizes the public service characteristic of the railway, and defines the administrative organization,
financial sources, internal controls and other organizational matters.
I RqilaW wagon an vetilatd car, sitable for bana boxes trnportation.



- 139-
Page S of 6
23.    FNH is run by a Board, integrated by a representative of SECOPT (who is the President), and
others from the Ministry of Economics, Ministry of Finance, Central Bank, Planning Ministry, Chamber
of Commerce of San Pedro Sula and the railroad Union. The General Manager is also the board
secretary. The company is organized into two regional divisions, each of them with a complete set of
departments: operations and maintenance, personnel, administration and fiar^e. All marketing and
commercial functions are missing. In the Valle de Sula sector there is a custom ser;: e department, which
must be utilized by all governmental import and exports in the region.
24.    The total staff of the FNH includes 340 people in the Valle de Sula sector, 139 in La Ceiba
sector and 140 retired at whom the company must make monthly payments. The company's management
is not business oriented, and with few exceptions does not seem to have adequate training for running '
railway on a commercial basis.
Summary
25.    The current situation of the FNH can be summarized as follows:
i The extent of the railway system is 886 km, but only 204 km, at Valle de Sula, are
operated by FNH; 336 km are transferred in concession for operation to a fruit company
(Tela Railroad Co.) and the remaining 346 km, included within FNH's system, are out of
service due to a severe deterioration of its infrastructure and no rail transport demand.
The system operated by FNH transports 332,000 tn of freight pe. year (during years
1989/90) of which 45% corresponds to the transport of bananas. This transport generates
11.6 millions of tn-km (55%) and the remaining freight 2.5 millions (45%). The average
distance is 77.3 km for bananas and 51.9 km for other products. For a railway system
these are reduced traffic densities and short distances.
FNH also transports passengers. During 1989/90 it carried 165,000 passengers per year,
generating 4.1 millions of passenger-km with an average distance of only 25 km per
passenger. In addition, FNH provides yard services moving TRR Co.'s wagons in Puerto
Cort6s.
The transportation of bananas defines the operational organization. The fruit exporters,
who are the main users of FNH, need to load the wagons in the production area (approx.
70/80 km south of Puerto Cortes) and rapidly transport them to the port in order to
coordinate the train and the ships arrival. Railway wagons should not stay in the port
terminal more than 24 hours (in no case more than 48 hours) to avoid fruit deterioration.
The freight is transported in 40' refrigerated containers loaded in flat cars; while at the
port they are connected to the power system of the Terminal constructed by TRR Co. The
main rail return freight is the transport of empty containers. Other main products are wood
(exported) and wheat (imported).
FNH in the Valle de Sula area has 10 locomotives, 521 wagons, 16 passenger cars and
other equipment. The engines have an average age of 27.5 years, but only 6 of them are
currently in use, 3 are used for freight trains, I for passenger trains and 2 are on yard
operations and make the reserve. The average utilization of equipment is 58,000 km/year,



- 140-
ANNEX 7
Page 6 of 6
relatively intensive for a short system. There is no programmed preventive maintenance
and the motive power is in poor condition. It must be pointed out that serious difficulties
arise with the wagons, with many derailments due to the transport of 40' containers on flat
cars not adequately prepared.
The track is also in bad shape and unsafe. Signals and communication systems are very
precarious. The repair shops have no adequate equipment and only make corrective
maintenance and repairs of the rolling stock.
The evolution of revenues and expenditures of FNH in the last 5 years shows an increasing
need of financial support. This is because of the following reasons:
* A complete sector (La Ceiba) has no revenues as it does not offer services any more,
but it kept until November 30, 1992, when all operational staff adhered to a voluitary
retirement program, a significant level of expenses to cover the payroll for 139
employees.
V Valle de Sula sector has been reducing its traffic level, and in 1990 was severely
affected because the freight rates have not been increased while devaluation reduced
the Lempira value by 165% since 1990.
"Other revenues" of La Ceiba sector were payments made by the Standard Fruit Co.
for each ton shipped carried, as a compensation for some repairs in La Tela-La Ceiba
rail done by FNH several years ago. As that section does not operate anymore, those
revenues have also been cut.
The significant deficit of 1990 was basically financed by the sale of real estate for 3.2
millions of Lps. and by Government support: 4 million Lps. It must be pointed out that
selling those lands made possible some additional repairs to the rolling stock, which are
included in the operational expenses of 1990.

HONDURAS
IMPLEMENTATION OF TRANSPORT PROJECTS
PAST BANK GROUP LENDING
1.01    Transport infrastructure in Honduras has significantly improved over the last four decades,
to a road network of highways and feeder roads that cover most of the country, and a port system that
more than satisfies the existing demand. Substantial institutional development also took place during
the period, with the strengthening of management capabilities in the roads and ports subsectors.
1.02   Those major infrastructure and institutional improvements were supported by eight highway
projects and three ports projects funded by the Bank group. Implementation of the eight highway
projects started in 1955, with the commencement of a Highway Maintenance Project, and ended in
1986 with the closing of the Eighth Highway Project. Port projects started in 1966 with the Puerto
Cortes project and ended in 1984 with the closing of the Third Port Project.
II. EXPERIENCE IN HIGHWAY PROJECTS
2.01    Bank involvement in the transport sector in Honduras started with Loan 135-HO for US$ 4.2
million approved in December 1955 for the Honduras Highway Maintenance Project. The main
objective of the project was the establishment and equipping of an effective highway maintenance
organization involving the reorganization of the Highway Department (Direcci6n General de
Caminos). To a great extent, the project resulted from the Bank's concern with the fact that
Honduras was embarking, with Bank support, in a substantial expansion of the main highway network
without having in place the necessary maintenance capability to ensure sustaLnability of the expected
beneflts. The project was successful in creating a more decentralized manance structure and
making inroads in the views of the Highway Department towards road maintenance.
2.02   In May 1958, the Bank approved the Highway Construction Project (Loan 195-HO; US$ 5.5
million) for the construction of a paved highway of 72 km from Bufalo to Puerto Cortes; the
construction of bridges and culverts on the Western Highway from Chemelecon to Santa Rosa de
Copan; pavement completion of the Southern Highway from Tegucigalpa to the Pan-American
Highway; preparation of final plans and specifications for the extension of the Western Highway
from Santa Rosa de Copan to the Salvadoran border. Overall, the project was successful in its main
objective of completing major sections of the road network.
2.03    The third commitment of the Bank Group came in 1961, with the approval of Credit 1-HO
for US$ 9.0 million for the Honduras Western Highway Extension and Supplementary Projects. The
project covered the construction of a 100 km unpaved highway from Santa Rosa de Copan to Nueva
Octepeque and the Salvadoran border, known as the Western Highway Extension; the continuation of



- 142 -
ANEX 
Page 2 of 6
the maintenance program for two years; a feeder road program for the Western Region; and a
planning survey of the highway system.
2.04    The civil work components under the project were completed successfully. The maintenance
program expanded previous efforts in road management to bridge maintenance and workshops
improvements. The results of the maintenance program improved with respect to that in the First
Highway Project, mainly because of an administration more sympathetic to road maintenance and
expatriate consultants. Finally, the highway survey, prepared by the Stanford Research Institute, was
instrumental in the preparation of subsequent highway projects.
2.05    The Bank Group financing of the Honduras North Road Project (Fourth Highway Project)
comprised a loan for US$ 6.0 million (Loan 400-HO) and a credit for US$ 3.5 million (Credit 71-
HO), both approved by the Board of Directors in February 1965. The objective of this project was
the construction of the principal central portion of the North Road, the backbone of the Honduras'
transport system, a 202 km two-lane highway linking Rio del Hombre and Potrerillos. Project
execution was delayed by changes in road alignment and unforeseen soil problems. The PCR
suggests that more frequent Bank supervision missions at the early stages of project implementation
would have shorten the decision periods and would have resulted in important cost savings.
Notwithstanding this qualifications, the project was extremely successful, generating traffic levels and
economic returns quite above appraisal expectations. Concerns for road maintenance were relegated
to covenants mainly related to budgetary allocations and axle load regulations. Although there was
progress in these areas, it was always below that envisaged at appraisal.
2.06   In May 1967 the Bank approved Loan 495-HO for US$ 8.6 million for the Honduras
Western Highway Paving Project (Fifth Highway Project). The project included improvements on the
Western Highway reconstructing 37 km between Chemelecon and La Entrada and paving 247 km
from Chemelecon to the Salvadoran border, and engineering studies for upgrading segments of the
main road network and expanding the feeder roads system. Again the Loan Agreement included
covenants obligating the Government to continue support for highway maintenance and participating
in the axle-load Central-American road user study. Furthermore, as part of the project, the Highway
Maintenance Department was established as and independent office of the Ministry of
Communications and Public Woiks. The project was completed successfully with only marginal cost
and time overruns.
2.07    The Sixth Highway Project was financed by Bank Loan 896-HO of May 1973, for US$ 18.8
million. The main objectives were the construction of the Tegucigalpa-Talanga road, upgrading 80
km of feeder roads and increasing SECOPT's capabilities in the areas of transport planning and road
maintenance. Included in the Sixth Highway Project were feasibility and engineering studies for
feeder roads totalling about 300 km. As part of the institution-building aspects, technical assistance
was provided to three government agencies involved in transport planning (CONSUPLAN, and
SECOPT's General Directorate for Transport and the Sectoral Planning Office). The major
achievement was the preparation of a much needed transport law enacted in 1976 to implement
regulations for enforcement of vehicle weights and dimensions. In addition, this technical assistance
laid the ground work and provided the basic data needed to prepare a Highway Master Plan under the
follow-on project. Extensive training to field road maintenance staff was also provided under the
project.



- 143 -
ANNEX 8
Page 3 of 6
2.08   In November 1976 the Bank approved loans 1341-HO and 1342-T-HO for a total of US$35
million for the Honduras Seventh Highway Project. The main objective of the project was the
reconstruction of the Talanga-Juticalpa-Catacainas road, and technical assistance for road planning and
maintenance. The latter included the preparation of a highway master plan, which identified
investment priorities financed under the Eighth Highway Project; continuation of the technical
assistance to increase the planning capacity of DGT and DOP; training to the Directorate General for
Roads (DGC) in the areas of laboratories' organization and equipment; the introduction of a system
for road maintenance and equipment management in a Pilot District; and a study to identify major
bottlenecks faced by the construction industry.
2.09    The civil works under the project were completed at a cost below appraisal estimates, which
allowed the inclusion under the project of access roads to the main corridor. Efforts to strengthen
transport planning capabilities, however, were considered less than satisfactory. The consultants
attributed poor results to lack of qualified staff, while the Borrower was of the idea that difficulties
were due to the provision of technical assistance to separate departments within SECOPT, instead of
focusing on a coordinated planning unit. The project also included funds for financing, with the
participation of the German Government, a pilot project to develop labor-based road construction
technologies. This element of the project was extremely successful and set the basis for a program
that continues to be a model for labor intensive road works. Implementation of a road maintenance
system in a pilot district was also satisfactorily completed. The expansion of the zYstem to the rest of
the country, however, was postponed because the consultants were required to prepare the Eighth
Highway Project.
2.10   In August 1980, a Loan 1901-HO for US$28 million was approved by the Bank for the
Honduras Eighth Highway Project. The loan was closed in June 1987 with an unused balance of
US$ 4.6 million which was cance'ed. The main objectives of the project were the improvement of
access roads to and from rural areas and the continuation of the institution-building aspects undertaken
under previous projects to strengthen highway administration and maintenance. The project included:
construction of 350 km of feeder roads within selected agricultural valleys; improvement of
approximately 115 km of secondary roads; purchase of maintenance and workshop equipment, and
spare parts; and technical assistance for implementing a maintenance management system for
scheduling and controlling maintenance activities.
2.11    The Eighth Highway Project was successful in facilitating access to and from rural areas.
Most of the sub-projects were completed on schedule and at cost below appraisal estimates. ERRs
were generally below those calculated at appraisal, mainly because of a slow down in the Honduran
economy. The technical assistance components continued to improve SECOPTI's capability for
planning and programming maintenance activities through the creation of a data system for network
conditions and the implementation of a countrywide road maintenance system. The system, however,
never became fully operational. Problems with the quality of field information were to be tackled in
a follow-on operation, but the project did not materialize because of the interruption and subsequent
change in focus of Bank lending to Honduras.



- 144-
ANNEX 8
Page 4 of 6
m. EXPERIENCE IN PORTS PROJECTS
3.01   In 1966 the Bank approved the first loan for the port sub-sector, Honduras First Port
Project, Puerto Cortes, for US$ 4.8 million. Loan 463-HO was to be executed by the newly created
Empresa Nacional Portuaria. ENP was created in October 1965 to preside over the expansion of the
system. Previously, publicly-owned port facilities in Honduras were managed by the Directorate
General of Customs and Indirect Taxes under the Ministry of Economy and Finance. The Bank had
an important role in the creation and start-up operations of the autonomous ENP, helping to set up its
organization, management and financial structure.
3.02 The project financed the construction of two deepwater berths and supporting facilities in
Puerto Cortes, Honduras main port. Even before the successful completion of these facilities in 1970,
it was evident that traffic was growing at a much faster rate than forecasted, and that further
expansion would be needed. Therefore, ENP commissioned consultants to perform a feasibility study
for the future capacity increase. Also, ENP investigated the feasibility of a deepwater port on the
Pacific Coast. The recommendations of these studies helped define the main components of the
Second Port Project.
3.03    Ti June 1971, the Bank approved Loan 767-HO for US$ 6.0 million for the Honduras
Second Port Project. The main components were the improvement and expansion of port facilities at
Puerto Cortes and the replacement of lighterage operations at Amapala by new deepwater operations
at San Lorenzo on the Pacific Coast. The new facilities would provide better and faster services
through mechanized cargo handling equipment. The San Lorenzo Port was justified at appraisal on the
basis of optimistic projections of westward sugar and molasses exports. Before the new facilities
were completed, however, it was decided to start with a second phase expansion of the port, which
was financed under the Third Port Project.
3.04    The Puerto Cortes component was satisfactorily completed providing Puerto Cortes with a
modern container handling facility for meeting the growing volume of containerized roll-on/roll-off
traffic. On the other hand, start-up of works at San Lorenzo experienced delays of about three years.
The main reason for the delay was the Government's indecision towards whether investments should
proceed in San Lorenzo or in Amapala and, subsequently, the Honduran Congress concern with the
true strategic value of a deep water facility in the Gulf of Fonseca. Finally, works started in 1975
and were completed in 1979 with a cost overrun of about 264%. Because of insufficient funds, the
Bank approved a supplementary loan of US$ 3.0 million.
3.05    The institution-building aspects of the project were successful. ENP became a well run,
appropriately staffed, operationally efficient and relatively autonomous enterprise with responsibility
for all port facilities in Honduras.
3.06    The Honduras Third Port Project was made with Bank's loans 1395-T-HO and 1396-HO for
US$12 million and IDA credit 696-HO for US$ 5.5 million, both approved on April 1977. The
objctives of the project were: to construct a new port in Puerto Castilla, on the Northeast Atlantic
Coast, to handle exports of timber and agricultural products related to an ambitious agricultural and
industrial development scheme in the Olancho region; and to complete the second phase of Puerto San
Lorenzo. The technical assistance and consulting services component of the projeet consisted on: a



- 145-
ANNEX 8
Page S of 6
study to determine the optimal depth of the San Lorenzo access channel; the preparation of a manual
detailing container operation procedures and documentation for Puerto Cortes; training for ENP staff
in container operations at Puerto Cortes; and consulting services for design and construction
supervision.
3.07    Actual total project cost was US$ 47.7 million (59.9% overrun). Very serious contractual
difficulties arose at Puerto Castilla. The contractor suspended work because of alleged frustration and
late payments; the case was arbitrated in favor of the contractor. After work was suspended the
Government and the Bank agreed to a reduction in the scope of the harbor works at Puerto Castilla
from three berths to one berth. One of the reasons for this reduction, was the increase cf the scope
of work at San Lorenzo to include channel dredging at an extra cost of US$ 4.4 million. The trends
that begun at San Lorenzo under the Second Project were even more pronounced under the Third
Project. Traffic originally forecasted for San Lorenzo and Puerto Castilla had fallen far short of
appraisal expectations. Costs also were substantially above original estimates. The PCR
optimistically estimated ex-post economic rates of return (ERR) of 3% for San Lorenzo and 12% for
Puerto Castilla.
3.08    The technical assistance and the institution-building aspects were again completed
successfully. ENP's financial procedures were also improved under this project. However, the civil
works at San Lorenzo were clearly unjustified and traffic developments subsequent to the ex-post
evaluation showed that investments in Puerto Castdlla were premature.
CONCLUSIONS AND LESSONS LEARNED
4.01    The Bank's participation has been instrumental in developing the transport system of
Honduras. In the highway sub-sector there was a logical and realistic sequence of project components
throughout the eight loans. The first piojects concentrated in the construction of major sections of
highways, and the development of a road maintenance capacity in the country. Then the emphasis
turned to secondary and feeder roads improving access to and from rural areas. At the same time,
the institution-building aspects were always taken into consideration with numerous technical
assistance programs. In the ports sub-sector, afler a very successful first project providing Puerto
Cortes with much needed modern facilities, the subsequent projects, over-dimensioned the port
infrastructure of the country, generaing premature and unappropriated investments. However, the
institutional aspects were accomplished successfully and ENP became a well run and relatively
autonomous enterprise.
4.02    The main lessons to be learned from the highway projects, as stated in the PCR's and
PPAR's are to: (i) develop mure realistic projections during appraisal, in order to avoid cost and
traffic miscalculations caused by erroneous economic development assumptions; (ii) exercise closer
supervision during execution, to increase the participation in the modifications that the borrower
might be planning at an early stage; (iii) increase emphasis on the institution-building aspects and
secure prior Government commitment; (iv) identify and implement appropriate technologies to best
utilize labor-intensive construction; and (v) realistically assess the difficulties of establishing efficient
road maintenance in countries lacking adequately trained staff.



- 146-
ANNEX 8
Page 6 of 6
4.03    For the port sub-sector, the main lessons learned were: (i) to avoid the excessive reliance
on optimistic, target oriented planning forecasts for port traffic, that in the Third Port Project led to
premature and inappropriate investments; (ii) in projects with long gestation periods, the adequacy of
its size and scope should be monitored to adapt to changing circumstances; contracts should be
phased, when possible, to allow for such revisions; and, (iii) changes in the scope of a project which
substantially impact project cost and financing, should only be considered after the original physical
objectives are near completion and firm costs for those components underway can be assessed.
4.04    In retrospect, it is clear tiat the lessons contained in the PCRs and PPAR's for these projects
reflect the views of the time on the main sectoral issues. It is true that, in general, the highway
projects were very successful in developing the main road infrastructure of the country. Progress in
developing institutional capabilities for road planning and road maintenance, however, were always
described as below expectations. This was systematically attributed to the long-term character of
institutional development and the difficulties of implementing sophisticated systems in countries
lacking the necessary absorptive capacity. A fresher look at those problems, incorporating the lessons
learned in world-wide Bank Group operations, would point to lack of accountability and strong
political incentives to adopt inefficient schemes. This is more so when viewed in the context of the
overall "road maintenance problem" in the developing world.
4.05    The experiences derived from the three port projects are mixed. The Bank had an important
role in the development of ENP as an efficient organization presiding over the expansion of the port
system. The Bank also supported the much needed expansion and modernization of Puerto Cortes.
Participation in the construction of Puerto Castilla and Puerto San Lorenzo, however, proved to be
unwise. Investments in Puerto Castilla were part of a large and ambitious scheme to develop the
Olancho region. Rather fortuitously, it became evident that the projected demand was too optimistic
and the original project was revised to substantially reduce the port's capacity.
4.06    The opposite is true with Puerto San Lorenzo. The port was expected to be built in two
phases to support the export of sugar and molasses through the Pacific. Implementation of the second
phase started even before the completion of the first one. The PPAR for the Third Port Project
concluded that, at the time of the start of the second phase, there were already strong signals that the
projected exports were not going to materialize. There are two important lessons to be learned from
this experience: One refers to the high risks of the public sector to misallocate resources, due to lack
of accountability. A different public-private sector mix would have probably prevented wasteful
investments. The other lesson deals with the difficulties associated with uncertainties and demand
forecast. In the case of large, lumpy investments and in periods of low to moderate economic
growth, it could be less costly to wait for some signs of incipient congestion before initiating further
investments, than to risk long periods of overcapacity and deficitary operations.

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Supervision Plan
Staff Input for Project Launch and Mid-Course Review
1.   Given the project's comprehensive approach to sector reform, and the nature and scope of
sector investment proposed, extensive and close supervision would be required, especially during the
first two years of project implementation.
2.   A project launch workshop and yearly project review workshops will be organized, assisting
the staff of the implementing agencies develop a common understanding of the project's overall
development objectives, operational procedures, coordination needs and potential implementation
problems.
3.   The tentative supervision timetable and required Bank staffing is the following:

and planning required coordination among agencies, project

1994 sector investment plan.

4.   In sum, the Project Launch and Annual Project Implementation Review Workshops, and the
Mid-Term Evaluation would require a total of 34 staffweeks. In addition, two regular supervision
missions (one of one-week duration, and one of two weeks), e- -)rising the task manager and two
specialists, w ill be necessary. Thus, total field supervision woua amount to 82 staffweeks, and it is
reasonable to assume that office supervision will amount to an additional 40 weeks, for a total of 122
staff weeks during the life of the project, i.e. an average of 30.5 staffweeks per year.
5.   During the first two years of project implementation, r-pervision efforts will focus on
organization and team development with a view toward strengthening the PC's operational capacity.
The Project Launch worksLop will be followed by additional supervision input to familiarize selected
local staff with IDA's procurement, disbursement and auditing procedures and guidelines. It is
expected that IDA staff will assist SECOPT and the other executing agencies in prioritizing
investments as well as human resource allocation.



- 149-
ANNEX
Page 3 of S
Proposed Agenda for Annual and Mid-Term Implementation Reviews
6.   The annual project review-to be organized by the PC-will take place in June of each year,
beginning in 1993, with the participation of the PC, representatives of the executing agencies, and
IDA staff. Anmual reviews would focus on evaluations of progress against the implementation of the
Action Plans and would serve to update and revise as necessary the 1993-1996 Transport Sector
Investment Program and financing plans and to discuss issues of overall project management with
senior Government officials. The result of the annual review would be the clarification of the
following year's operating plan, identifying and agreeing on remedial actions required to address
problems of changing circumstances during implementation. Thus the annual review and planning
process will provide a tool for ongoing refinements in project strategies and plans, consistent with the
overall objectives of the project.
7.   The mid-term project review to be conducted no later than June 30, 199S would follow the
same format as the annual reviews but the analysis of project impact would be expanded. In addition,
the mid-term review will be result in a darified and agreed set of indicators for years 1996 through
project completion in 1997.
8.   A summary of the areas to be addressed in the annual and mid-term reviews is presented
below. Key indicators against which project performance would be measured, are presented in Table
1. Monitoring target dates for evaluating the progress in implementing the Government's Action
Plans are presented in Annex 1.
Sector-Wide Issues:
(a)   Sector Planng: Progress in the establishment of a functioning institutional framework
for the review of sector institutions and performance, the design and implementation of
an information network, and the provision of sector analyses, including environmental
concerns, relevant to program and policy decisions (Annex 1);
(b)  Reulator Framework: Progress in achieving a regulatory environment free of
unnecessary distortions, including the approval of a new transport law and the
elimination of urban transport subsidies that cannot be justified on equity grounds; and
(c)   Subsector Investment: Progress in implementing the Government's 1993-1996
Transport Sector Investment Plan and revision of the plan in view of progress achieved
in the previous year and possible changes in priority.
Road Subsector Issues:
(a) Progress in increasing SECOPT's accountability for road management through the
establishment of performance budgets (physical and financial targets for road
maitnance are included in Table 1);
(b)  Progress in continuing reduction of DGCCA staff Crable 1); and
(c) Progress in establishing a Bridge Management System (Annex 4).

(a)   Progress in decentralizing and privatizing port operations as we measured by reduction

Airport Subsector Issues:
(a) Progress in establishing an organizational structure to operate the airports on a

Railway Subsector Issues:
(a) Progress in phasing out FNH operations measured against targets to reduce FNH's staff
(Annex 7).

HONDURAS
TRANSPORT SECTOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Economic Evaluadon
1.   About 90% of the projects being considered for the 1993-1996 Transport Sector Investment
Program have feasibility studies either recently carried out or updated. The analysis of works in the
road rehabilitation and periodic maintenance component was based on a detailed inventory of the
existing paved roads and the results obtained from the application of the HDM III. About 259 links
totalling 5,546 km were evaluated, of which 2,400 km were identified for rehabilitation including
those to be financed under the project. Because of lack of timely maintenance, these roads are
entering the critical period when deterioration accelerates. The maintenance policies used in the
analyses were developed in collaboration with DGC and DGCCA and the unit costs of maintenance
operations used are reasonable for the region. Traffic volumes and compositions are based on actual
counts supplemented with origin/destination data. For each link, life-cycle costs were simulated,
based upon initial surface conditions (e.g., roughness and area cracked, number of years since last
periodic intervention, etc.), horizontal and vertical geometric characteristics, the road maintenance
policy in force and the volume and composition of traffic using the link. Thus, for each link, the
maintenance policy alternative yielding the l1-ast cost to society (minimum cost to the road authority
and the user) was identified. Tradeable goods were valued at their border prices. The opportunity
cost of unskilled labor was conservatively estimated as 80% of the market wage. The EER for these
works range from 12% to above 50%.
2.   During the first year of the proposed project, IDA would finance the rehabiitaiton of the
Choluteca-Guasaule and the Tela-Ceiba roads, both with an estimate ERR of 21 %. Tables 1 and
Graphs 1 to 2 present, respectively, the cost-benefit streams for the proposed works, the changes in
roughness (and therefore in vehicle operating costs) derived from the application of the selected
maintenance policy, and the sensitivity analysis. The estimated ERRs are robust, since a 20%
increase in the investment costs together with a 20% reduction in the benefits still yields an EER of
about 14%.
3.   In the case of feeder roads, the economic justification was based on the evaluation of the entire
network (8,619 km), and more than 1,500 km were identified for rehabilitation or periodic
maintenance, with ERRs also ranging from 12% to above 50%. The analysis of these roads was
based on the producer surplus method, for which the value of the additional agricultural production
attributed to the reduction in transport costs was quantified. Under the current investment financing
constraints, however, not all feeder road projects with a positive net present value that also meet the
agreed technical and environmental criteria can be executed. The final selection was made on the
basis of the export corridor concept, selecting groups of roads feeding the main traffic arteries.
Supporting studies for the road works in the Program would be entered in the Project File.



- 154-
ANNEX11
Page 2 of 2
4.   The project also includes IDA financing of rehabilitation works in the La Mesa Airport (San
Pedro Sula). La Mesa is the primary international airport in Honduras and services both civilian and
military aircraft including Boeing 727's, Boeing 737's, and Lockheed C-13's. It has approxiBately
265,000 square meters of air-side pavements consisting of a 2,734 meter long by 45 meter wide
runway, a near full length parallel taxiway, five connecting taxiway, a military apron, and a
commercial apron. The approximate age of the pavements is about 20 years, with a number of
asphaltic concrete overlays having been put down in the last ten years. Much of the existing
pavement is presently in poor condition as a result of age, traffic, inadequate drainage, poor native
subgrade conditions, and inconsistent original construction. Both medium and heavy load traffic is
presently being restricted at La Mesa Airport. Extensive rehabilitation works are proposed to upgrade
the existing pavements to handle the increasing traffic at the airport (in terms of both volume and axle
loadings). Two possible alternatives were considered for the rehabilitation of the runway: one was to
completely remove all failed pavements at La Mesa Airport and to reconstruct the pavement using
new sand subbase, gravel base, and asphaltic concrete (AC) surface courses; the other was to carry
out the upgrading using Portland Cement Concrete (PCC) rigid pavement design, constructed where
practical over the existing pavement structure. The latter was selected for the following main
reasons: (i) the runway complex at La Mesa Airport must be kept in operation during upgrading.
The use of overlay PCC construction will not only reduce the time of construction, but will allow for
extensive phasing of the work items; and (ii) the subgrade at La Mesa Airport is weak and susceptible
to disturbance due to construction traffic and inclement weather (rain, etc). Procedures which do not
expose the subgrade would be preferred.
5.   The economic evaluation of the proposed investments to rehabilitate the runway in the San
Pedro Sula Airport is based on benefits accruing to air transport users due to: (i) reduction of
airplane costs for national and international traffic derived from improved runway and taxiway
surfaces; and (ii) savings due to improved operating costs in the "with" project case. Costs are based
on final enginering studies carried out by the US Corps of Engineers in 1989 and updated in early
1992 by a local consulting firm. Traffic growth was estimated at 6% per year for the 1995-1996
period and 4% per year thereafter. The proposed investment has an estimated EER of 20%.
Supporting studies would be entered in the Project File.
